SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
Credit and Project Sunmmary
Borrower:                  Government of Rwanda
Beneficiary:               Ministry of Agriculture, Livestock and Forests
Amount:                    SDR 11.0 million (US$14.1 million equivalent)
Terms:                     Standard IDA terms
On-lending Terms:          Up to 20% of the proceeds of the *. edit would he on-lent to four
project revenue-earning entities, namely the CAT, Veterinary
pharmacy, Beef fattening facility. and dairy to consolidate
investments begun. in the first project; to pasture beneficiaries
to complete pasture establishment and herd constitution; and to
farmers in Gishwati to develop the degraded parts of the Gis:lwati
forest. The funds would be onlent at the IBRD rate for 20 years
including five years grace. The beneficiaries of the on-lending
will bear the fo .-ign exchange risk.
Project Description:  The Projecc's ol.jectives are to: (a) protect and conserve two of
Rwanda's remaining natural forestc of the Zaire-Nile divide; (b) promote agroforestry as
a cost-effective means of inc.reasing fuelwood supplies; (c) consolidate investments made
in the first forestry project in plantations and pastures and, (d) continue to reinforce
the capacity of the Ministry of Agriculture to plan, supervise and monitor forestry
sector programs. The project would assist the Ministry to better integrate project
activities into its normal framework; to improve management of the first prcject's
investments in pastures and plantations and, where suitable to transfer such management
to the private sector.
The project would be implemented over a six-year period including a one year
baild-up phase and would comprise: (a) protection and conservation of the Nyungwe and
Gishwati natural forests through the development of buffer plantations, an ecological
itnventory and a natural resources monitoring and .valuaticn program; (b) promotion of
farm fotestry and rural afforestation tirroLgh the strengthening of seedling production
and distribution, implementation of a rut.l afforestation mssonitoring and evaluation
program, and training of higher level foresters and other forestry workers;
(c) inte?-ited forestry, agric.,lture and livestock development in Gishwati including
consolidation of investments in pas':ures and plantations made under the first project
and incre*ased private sector participation in management of the livestock activities;
development of an additional 2,000 ha o' protection plantations on denuded forest
slopes; sjpport for a land use stud; and for new approaches to land management on
degraded forest areas and intensification of extension services around the forest;
(d) maintenance of fuelwood and pole short rotation plantations developed In the first
project; (e) applied forestry and animal nutritfoni research; and (f) pilot pasture
rehabilitation in the Mutara region.
Bcneftrs and Risks. The project would provide economy-wide ecological and hydrolog-
ical benefits from protection and conservation of the Nyungwe and Gishwati forests.
There would be institutional development benefits from Improved ministry lev2l plan-
r.ing, monitoring and evaluation of forestry programs. The project would produce
incremental fuelwood and industrial wood, meat, dairy products and foodcrops with a vi'ce
distribution of benefits to over 75,000 farm families, mnost of them In absolute poverty.
The primary risk relates to Governmenti's abilitv to carry out proposed management
reforms in Glshwati including a greater role for the private sector.  The Government has
shown its corinitment by having begun mreasures to implement these reforms.

RWANDA
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
I. BACKGROUND
A. Project Background
1.01     'he proposed project would be the second in support of integrated
forestry developmenz in Rwanda. The objecti es of the first project
(Credit 1039 RW) due to close June 30, 1987 4ere to: (a) develop an
institutional capacity for policy formulation and implementation in the
forestry sector of Rwanda, (b) initiate a reforestation program to provide
fuelwood, poles and saw timber for the country and (c) develop and sustain
the exi.ting livestock industry in the Gishwati forest area ill western
Rwanda for dual purpose meat ar.d milk production. This development was to
follow an integrated approach involving conservation of forest resources.
1.02     Implementation of the first project has not been without its
problems. Along with support from other donors, principally the S, 'ss, the
project has laid the groundwork for forest policy formulation in Rwanda and
succeeded in achieving physical targets for plantation and pasture
development. It has, however, had less success in attaining some of its
.onservation end production objectives.  Uncontrolled livestock and human
activity continue to be a problem in the undegraded portions of the
Gishwati forest. There are alsc problems with growth rates in the Kigali-
Butare fuelwood plantations where, as a result of the poor sites, projected
wood output will fall below expectations. Managerial and institutional
issues have moreover arisen as a result of the inadequate superv4Aion of
the project unit by the project management committee.
1.03     At tbC start of the first project, Government's thinking on
forestry sector policy issues was at the initial stages. Since then, a
numDer of important developments have taken place. With funding from the
first proJect and assistance from other donors, the Governmert has
developed a forest policy and laws which provide a framework within which
specific actions can be implemented. The Natural Forest Conservation and
Management Plan (Plan d'Action pour la Conservation et l'Amenagement des
Forets Naturelles de la Crete Zaire-Nil, PACZN) adopted in 1984 spells out
a policy and management action plan tc protect the rapidly diminishing
natural forests of the Zaire-Nile Crest in the west. The Forestry Law
("Loi forestiere") soon to be promulgated, sets out the legal framework for
future forest, development including strengthening of the policing powers of
the Forestry Department (DGF) and establishment of a Forestry Development
fund (Fonds Forestier National, (FFN). The Governmant is also formulating
(in t:he context of its 1987-1991 4th national development plan) a forestry
su;b-sector plan and investment program which will provide the basis for
future donoc support to the sector. Concurrent with these developments in
the forestry sector there has also been an increase in farm forestry
through spontaneous planting of farm trees. Farm forestry has thus become
one of -he fastest growing and most promising avenues for future fuelwood
procut() .1  so:



- 2 -
1.04     The proposed project would build on these developments and on the
experience gained in the first project. It would: (a) consolidate and
maintain investments in plantations and pastures made in the first project;
(b) support the development of new activities in forest protection and
rtvral afforestation; and (c) continue to reinforce the Ministry of
Agriculture by improving its planning, supervision a..d monitoring capacity
in particular, of forestry sector ?rograms, and gradually phase out
management and implementation of project activities by the existing proiect
unit. The main thrust of the project's new activities would be the
protection, conservation and management of Rwanda's remaining natural
forests. The project would also support the development of farm forestry
as a more cost-effective way of increasing fuelwood supplies. Through the
proposed project activities, the Government would be assisted in
implementing and further developing its forest policy.
1.05     The project was prepared in the course of thrr  missions, the last
in September 1985 by an FAO/CP team with the input of Rwandese officials.
The present report is based on the findings of IDA missions which visited
Rwanda in April/May 1986 and September 1986. During appraisal, the scope
and orientation of the project w4as substantially changed from the proposals
in the preparation report to: (a) strengthen institution building within
DGF and the ministry; (b) encourage farm forestry; (c) expand the scope of
natural forest protection and management; (d) reorient the approach to
integrated forestry and livestock development in Gishwati with a view to
correcting problems arising Dut of the first phase; and (e) encourage a
more rational approach to the delivery of extension services in the
communes around the Gishwati forest. A new component to rehabilitate
deteriorating pastures in the Mutara region was added.
B. Economic and Agricultural Setting
1.06     RwanJa has a solid record of development achievements in the post-
independence period but faces deep-seated problems mostly related to the
pressures of population on scarce land resources. Located far from major
transportation routes in the very heart of Africa, and with a poor natural
resou.ce base, its per capita income is less than $300 a year. Average
annual rural family income (1985 prices) is about US$600 or $80 per capita.
Agriculture is the backbone of Rwanda's economy, contributing about 46% of
GDP at current prices and 83% of the total value of export.s. As much as
95% of the population is in the rural areas and is engaged in smallholder,
rainfed agriculture. Agricultural production is directed mainly toward
meeting domestic food needs. Of vital importanLe, however, is production
of export crops, notably coffee, tea, and cinchona which supply most of
Rwanda's foreign exchange and rural cash income. Until recently, growth of
agricultural output was relatively strong (5.8% per annum in the period
1974-80) and food production appears to have kept pace with population
growth.  Between 1980-83, agricultural output growth slowed down to an
average annual rate of about 3.5% less than the estimated population growth
rate of 3.7%. After the 1984 drought in which Rwanda experienced
substantial crop failures, growth of agricultural output appears to have
picked up and is now estimated at about 4% per annum. Some food is
imported commercially, mainly wheat, rice, oils, salt and sugar. Food aid



- 3 -
flows in recent years have been in the order of 10,000 to 15,000 tons of
cereals, beans, powdered milk and vegetable oils. Total food imports in
1985 amounted to about US$56 million or 16% of the total value of
merchandise imports.
1.07     With its population of 6.0 million or 250 persons per km2 (400 per
kmZ of arable land) Rwanda is the most densely settled country in
continental Africa. The high population growth rate means that densities
are inexorably rising. There is no longer much scope for increasing the
area under cultivation. Expan~sion and intensification of cropping without
adequate management is posing an increasing threat to soil fertility now
regarded as among the most important constraints to increasing agricultural
productivity in most areas of Rwanda. A measure of the mounting pressure
of people on land is the decline of fallow land: only 12.5% of arable land
is in fallow, down from 16% only 10 years ago. Rwanda is overwhelmingly a
country of small farmers: over half the one million farm holdings are less
than two hectares. The fragmentation of holdings (and the attendant desire
for more land) coupled with overgrazing by lives-.ock, have contributed to
severe deforestation and some soil erosion. Although the effects of the
latter are mitigated by a nation-wide drlve to introduce soil protection
measures, deforestation is a source of grave concern as it threatens
watersheds, land and soil resources. A high priority must therefore be
placed on arresAing deterioration of the natural resource base upon which
development will ultimately depend. The Government and the farmers are
taking some action to stop soil erosion, and protect natural resources,
through planting of trees and other measures. The will exists to do more.
This can be harnessed in the short to medium term ty applying solutions
that are pretty well proven in the fields of agro-forestry and natural
forest protection. Longer term solutions must, however, rely on results
emerging from applied agricultural and forestry research even thougi
dividends may well not pay off for at least a decade.
C. Government's Agricultural Sector Strategy
1.08     The focus of Government's agricultural sector strategy continues
to be the increase of farm level production in order to achieve food self-
sufficiency and balanced development of the export crop sector.
Government's objectives and strategy are spelled out in a recent (May 1986)
strategy paper on the reorientation of agricultural projects. The paper
outlines the framework for a Food Strategy, the major objective of which is
the achievement of food self-sufficiency based on production not only for
subsistence but also for the market. Production increases in the food and
export crop sectors would come through the intensified use of inputs -
fertilizer, selected seeds, mane:e, and careful management of the natural
resource base.
i.09     Another important objective of the strategy is the need to ensure
more coherence among the multiplicity of donor-financed development
projects which serve as the main instruments for implementation of Rwanda's
agriculture sector programs. The inconsistencies in approach and thc
different structures of intervention introduced in the projects are cited
as an important reason for their poor performance. Government aims to put
greater emphasis on adoption of instituLional structures that are
sustainable after the project and on affordability of project investments.



- 4 -
1.10     Gover,ment has had a tradition of relative non-intervention in the
food crop sector but has been rather "dirigiste" with regard to the e;port
crop sector and to other subsectors such as livestock and dairy
development. There are also temptations to intervene more in areas such as
marketing and input supply. However, the drive to have affordable and
sustainable investments is recognized as ar important check to the degree
of Government invovement.
1.11     The Government has made important progress over the last few years
in defining a sector strategy. It recognizes that in order to achieve
sectoral objectilves, it will have to improve its planning and management
capacity; reorient agricultural research to serve the traditional farm
system; and strengthen the links between reseaLr'a and extension. It will
also have to improve the organization of field extension services; increase
the availability of agricultural inputs and facilitate the involvement of
the private sector in their supply; strengthen the agricultural marketing
system and increase the supply of technical and managerial manpower.
Effective actions wculd also have Lo be taken to address issues of land
conservation and management, including soil erosion prevention and
protection of natural forests which are increasingly being depleted for
fuelwood and sawlogs.
1.!2     A policy framework hes beeni developed for the forestry sector,
but refinements still have to be made and concrete actions  o implernent the
policies have to be fully worked out. Past Government eff zts in the
forestry sector have focused on the development of state plantations to
serve urban energy and industrial wood needs. Although the need still
remains to find solutions to urban energy requirements, there is a greater
willingness to emphasize development of farm forestry and promotion of
private and communal initiatives in rural afforestation.  New directions
are also now being pursued to protect and conserve the ccuntry's natural
forests and to find solutions to the high levels of human and livestock
activities in some of these forests. The Government recognizes the
important interaction at the farm level of forestry, livestock and cropping
activities and the need to develop these in an integrated nanr-!r.
D. IDA'S Assistance and Strat!gy
1.13     Since 4.ts start in 1973, IDA lending for Rwanda's agricultural
sector has amounted to US$92.4 million for eight operations, of which five
are ongoing. Briefly, these operations are: (a) four area development
projects In the eastern Savannah zone (US$42.2 million); (b) two area
development projects in the Lake Kivu and Zaire-Nile Divide zones (US$36
million); (c) a Cinchona Development Project ($1.8 million); and (d) an
Agricultural Research Projec.t, (US$11.5 million'.  A US$22 million project
to support agricultural institutional development and field level extension
services (GitaramlLMinagri Project, Credit 1669 RW) was recently signed.
1.14     IDA (like other donors) has tended in the past to support projects
of an "integrated rural development" nature. Completion repozts have been
prepared for the first Mutara Agricultural Development Project (Credit 439
RW), the Bugesera-Gisaka-Migongo Pha,? I project (Credit 668 Rw) and the



- 5 -
Cinchona Project (Credit 656 RW). Experience from these and other on-going
projects highllght a number of issues affecting project performance,
namely: (a) poor iderntification and adaptation of appropriate technical
packages due to ir.sufticient knowledge of Rwanda's complex farming systems;
(b) inadequate mrlonlitorinig of project progress; (c) project complexity and
lack of training and experience of Rwandese management and
staff; (d) excessive diversity of pLoject institutional and organizational
systems mace un-;-anageable by weak agrihultural planning, project
preparation, financial curoJ.Lo  and weak technical central ministry
departmet:ts; and (e) scar:city of technical messages for agriculture

problems arising out of the first phase. The proposed project would

higher levels of cost recovery for pasture investment and operating costs
and the acquisition of appropriate equipment for the dairy. Managemeibt of
pasture and dairy activities would be decentre_ized to improve efficiency.
A land use study to address environmental problems would be supported.
IDA's Sectoral dialogue and Lending S_tratgy
1.18     As a result of lessons emerging from the first project and
experience from other projects IDA's sectoral dialogue and lending strategy
has evolve<. In-depth discussions with the Government have led to
recommendations to a mole away from dependence on project implementation
units and exclusive focus on integrated rural development type projects.
GOR and IDA have agreed to focus more directly on the development of
national approaches to sectoral probl.oms, by improving the policy framework
and investment strategy, including investment planning and management.
Attention would be given to institution-building to strergthen ministry
level capacity for planning, monitoring and evaluation, financial budgeting
and control, and training. Other priority areas retained for medium-term
focus and lending include development of the Government's agricultural
services essential for agricultural intensification; development of private
sector services for marketing, input supply and agricultural processing;
and identification and implementation of technical training priorities.
1.19     IDA has recently financed two oper  s4.ons which will fully support
the above sector strategies as they address .y issues of agricultural
services - extenslon and research, and sectoral management. The
Agricultural Research Project (Credit 1546 RW) is designed to address
research issues at the national level.  The Gitarama/Minagri Project
(Credit 1669 RW) will be a key instrument for reinforcing the ministry's
capability to stimulate agricultural production, employment and income
through the strengthening of extension and other agricoltural services and
the promotion of private sector initiatives. Similarly, the proposed
project would reinforce the implementation of national forestry policy
focusing on key areas of forest protection and prr,iotion of agroforestrv.
The project vould strengthen DGF's capacity to plan, monitor and evaluate

forestry sector programs and modest beginnings will be made to rationalize

II. FORESTRY SECTOR
A. Background and Role in the Economy
2.0:     Rwanda's forests play a vital role in the economy and farming
systems. Wood provides about 95% of total energy requirements. The
natural forests play an important role in protection of upper watersheds
for a large part of the country as well as being important reserves of
Afrc-montane flora and fauna. At the farm level, trees form an important
part of traditional agro-forestry farming systems. They provide fruits,
tree fadder, building poles, mulch, fix nitrogen and reduce soil erosion.
Trees planted along farm boundaries reduce wind velocity and improve soil
moisture retention, both of which have a positive impact on crop yields.
Data on Rwanda's forest resources are weak due to the lack of a
comprehensive national survey. However, according to the latest (end 1984)
official estimates, forest vegetation covered an area of 656,000 ha or 25%
of the country's 'and area. If dispersed tre-s planted on farms and fallow
land and equivalent to a forested area of 68,300 ha are added, total forest
vegetation comes to 724,000 ha or 28% fo Rwanda's land aria.1
2.02     There are four remaining natural forests situated at elevations
between 1,700 and 4,500 meters above sea level along the Zaire-Nile Divide:
Nyungwe (97,000 ha), Mukura (2,000 ha), Gishwati (21,900 ha), and Birunga
(15,000 ha). The importar.t ecclogical and hydrological role of these
forests has led the Government to declare them reserves with the aim of
controlling activities such as mining, pitsawing, firewood gathering and
farning. In some of the forests population pressure coupled with heavy
livestock activity and ineffective protection has resulted in their
increased exploitation, degradation and reduction in size and quality
(Table 2.1). With the 6Aception of the Akagera National Park (267,000 ha),
and the adjacent Mutara nature reserve (L.,000 ha) the savannahs which
cover slightly more than half of the country's forest land are rapidly
disappearing mainly through illegal cutting to supply Kigali with charcoal.
The Government accepts that at the current rate of extraction, the
savannahs outside of these two reserves will probably disappear in the next
3-5 years and is trying to prevent si.,i±ar exploitation of the remaining
natural forests.

an equivalent forested area of up to 200,000 ha - for example, see
Winterbottom, Robert, "Rwanda Integrated Forestry and Livestock
PLoJect", Pre-preparation Mission, August 1985.

B. Consumptpon<_Product otn and Marke i_, of Wood
2.03     According to Government statistics total wood consumption at the
end of 1981 amounted to 4.9 mlllion m3 per annum or 0.91 m3 per capita per
year.  About 95% of this was for domestic energy consumption.  Estimates of
annual wood production from the different forest types are uncertain due to
different assumptions about yield and area.  Official estimates indicate a
figure of about 2.1 million m3 implying a deficit in production of about
2.8 million m3 per year.  Other sources put production figures much higher.
For example, the Winterbottom report3 on the incidence and potential of
farm forestry in Rwanda (based on surveys and estimates) indicates
production figures pos3ibly as high as 4.7 million m3. Most of this
nroduction is for fuelwood.  The wide divergence between produc*ion
estimates underlines the naucity of hard information in this area. There
is reason to believe that Gov:ernment estimates are on the low side because
of inadequate accounting for produc:ion fromn farm forestry.  The success of
farm forestry means that production deficits as estimated above tray be much
lower underlying the need for caution with programs aimed at establishing
new state fuelwood plantations pa ticularly since the latter are much less
cost effective than farm forestry. Production deficits are usually made up
by overcutting of plantations and natural forests and use of crop residues
at the expense of maintaining soil fertility.  With projected rapid
population increase and the attendant i-,crease in domestic ener,;y
requirements, measures should continue to intensify rural tree planting
while protecting the natural forests. These reasures should, however,
focus on encouragement of agroforestry, as proposed under the project.

C. 3overnment's Forestry Sec.or Policies
2.07     The Government's sector objectives and strategy are spelled out in
the draft forestry sector's five year development plan paper (1987-91) and
in the May 1986 strategy paper on the reorientation of agricultural
projects. The broal.. objectives are to achieve an equilibrium between the
demand and supply of forest products while maintaining an optimam amount of
forest cover for ecc.ogical purposes. The strategy for achieving these
objectives includes: introduction of a system of protection, conservation
and management of existing forest resources, continued reafforestation and
encouragement of agroforestry; improved maintenance of already reforested
areas, better integration of trees in agriculture and livestock farming
systems, iwiproved energy efficiency, better market.,g of forest products
and '.ncreased training of forestry personnel and entrepreneurs.
2.08     The policy framework for implementing the above strategy is
provided by PACZN and the "Loi forestiere" PACZN cjalls for the
establishment of reserves in the remaining natural forests, development of
buffer plantations and other protective measures, implementation of a
forest inventory and implementation of forest management activities. The
"Loi Forestiere" provides guidelines for the development and implementation
of all forestry activities including the enforcement of protection
regulations, control of the harvesting and exploitation of wood, procedures
for the classification and management of forest lands allocated to the
state, cormnunes (local government), and private sector; provision for the
development of national and communal forestry plans t:o define sectoral
goals and the organization of an FFN to finance forestry activities. The
Loi Forestire emphasizes the need for communes and the private sector to
play an increasing role in the development of the forestry sector. The
capacitv of the communes to participate in the forestry sector is, however,
at present severely limited by the lack of funds and techn.4cal expertise.
A World Bank study on the role of Rwanda's communes in rural 2evelo ment
should shed some light on how to involve conmnunes more effective-y.
2.09     Rwanda has made tremendous strides in setting out a policy
framework, strategy and specific targets for developzent of the forestry
sector. In this regard Rwanda is far ahead of many African countries.
Still more could be acliieved by refining action plans and focusing on cost
effective solutions to forest production, protection and conservation.
D. Sector Organization and Management
2.10     DGF is in charge of forestry activities.  It is quite new having
been established in 1983 with funding from the first phase IDA project
Credit 1039 RW and assistance from the Swiss. DGF's general
responsibilities are: the formulation and application of national forest
policy; the preparation and implementation of national forestry plans; the
preparation, monitoring and control of forestry projects and forest
4/ The study, begun September 1986, is being carried out by the Programs
division.



- 11 -
legislation and regulation of harvesting. DGF faces a shortage of trained
personnel. There are at present 23 forest engineers, 50 forest technicians
and 120 village level forestry personnel. This is less than a quarter of
the staff estimated by the agency as necessary to enable it to effectively
carry out its mandate. Local training of forest technicians by the Swiss
and training of higher level foresters through the proposed project should
help to improve the situation.
2.11     In additicn to DGF, other important actors in the forestry sector
include co munles (local goverrunents) which own and man..ge over 40,000 ha of
plantations. The Rwandese Tourism Office controls over 300,000 ha of
national parks and the tea and pyrethrum parastatals (OCIR The, Office du
Pyrethre) and other companies have established fuelwood plantation to
supply thefr energy and other wood needs. Of great importance are the
actions of over twenty donozr-assisted development projects that either have
a forestry component or are focused mainly on forestry.  About 35,000 ha of
state plantations have been established through these projects. The
projects play an important role in integrated forestry development and in
institutional development of the forestry sector. Among the many
multilateral and bilateral donors the Swiss have had the greatest impact on
the forest sector particularly in training and policy fornulation. Non-
governmental organizations such as CARE International and Africare are also
beginning to assume some importance. The natural forest protection
component would involve four external aid agencies:  CCCE (France), Swiss
Aid, FED and IDA in parallel financing of the component.
E. Sector Funding
2.12     Government funding for forestry sector activities has only been
clearly separated from funding for other agricultural sector activities
from 1984. About 12% of the US$14 million agriculture sector budget is
allocated to forestry on the average each year. Annual appropriatior.s 'rom
the recurrent budget (budget ordinaire) amount to about RF 115 million
(US$1.4 million) and from the development budget RF 32 million (US$0.4
million). This is in contrast to financing from external sources estimated
- average about RF 605 million (US$7.3 million) per annum and channelled
for the most part through the various development projects. External
funding is the driving force behind the development of the forestry sector
and will continue to be for some time to come.  The Government is however
becoming increasingly aware of the :.eed to develop local sources of
financing and has (with Swiss assistance) created the FFN, as an
extrabudgetary mechanism to channel receipts from exploitation of
Government plantations along with other monies accruing to the forestry
sector. Such monies could be increased by introducing appropriate policies
regarding stumpage fees and a measure of direct cost recovery for seedlings
in the 1,500 government and project-supported village nurseries. The
proposed project would assist DGF to initiate a gradual and modest program
of cost recovery focused mainly on beneficiaries who do not contribute to
nursery establishmient costs_



- 12 -
F. Main Sectoral Issues
2.13     The lack of a rational forest policy was regarded in the past as
the main constraint to development of the forestry sector. Now, a broad
policy framework is in place along with necessary instrumients. However,
the means to implement the policies are still a major problem, as is the
delineation of responsibilities for management of the sector. Key
questions relate to the role of the regular line agency DGF in sector
management, the role of Government parastatals, donor-assisted developmernt
projects and the private sector. With these various actors and with DGF's
limited capacity to monitor, evaluate and coordinate project activities,
there is a danger of disparate and conflicting policies. Clearly, there is
a need to strengthen DGF's monitoring and evaluation capa ;ity and thereby
its analytical and planning capability.
2.14.    Another major problem facing the forestry sector is the general
critical shortage of agricultural land and how to manage the competing
demands of forestry, agriculture and livestock activities. Livestock
rearing has increasingly encroached on forest land while the planting of
farm trees competes with the need for agricultural land. Recent studies
suggest the possibility of building on traditional agro-forestry techniqaes
to better integrate forestry with agriculture and livestock rearing. The
present densities of less than 50 trees per ha of farmland are relatively
low and could be increased by up to 300Z with selected species that perform
multiple functions including prevention of soil erosion, improvement of
soil nutrient content, provision of fodder for livestock and wood for fuel.
The challenge facing the forestry sector is the development and
dissemination of these relevant species through appropriate nursery
development policies and improved agro-forestry research.
2.15     The very weak information base on the supply, demand pricing and
commercial,zation of wood is an important issue for the forestry sector.
The reliability of official estimates of wood production and consumption is
often called into question and very little information exists on wood
pricing and marketing (para. 2.04). There is limited monitoring of the
changing dynamics of wood supply and demand. The situation may be more
serious for some categories of wood than for others. For example, there
are suggestions of a current and projected oversupply of pine (used for
sawnwood) in the medium term. While the production of sawnwood can be
estimated with some accuracy due to known plantation establishment rates,
the picture is unclear with regard to demand. There is clearly a need for
improvement in the collection of information. The proposed scudy on wood
pricing and marketing would improve the information base for policy
decisions related to forest products. Future monitoring of market
conditions for various categories of wood would be included in the proposed
monitoring and evaluation program for rural afforestation.



- 13 -
III. THE PROJECT
A. Rationale
3.01     The project would provide a major impetus to the implementation of
Government policies for the protection and conservation of Rwanda's
remaining natural forests and the promotion of agro-forestry to increase
fue.wood supplies. The project would build on the experience accumulated
during the first phase to assist the Government in carrying out difficult
but innovative institutional reforms involving the progressive phasing out
of project implementation by project units and assumption of this function
by the ministry structure or, where suitable, by the private sector. The
project would further serve as a means for improving management and
implementation of programs begun in the first project.
3.02     The project would consist of (a) completion and maintenance of
investments in plantations and pastures made in the first project; (b)
continuation and strengthening of activities which have proved successful
in the first project and reorientation of others which have experienced
implementation problems; and (c) development of new activities in forest
protection and agro-forestry. A summary description of proposed project
activities is given below.
B. Summary ProJect Description
3.03     The project would include:
(a) protection and conservation of the Nyungwe and Gishwati
natural forests through the development of buffer
plantations, support for an ecological inventory and a
natural resources monitoring and evaluation program.
(b) support to agroforestry and rural afforestation through the
strengthening of seedling production and distribution,
support for a modest cost recovery program for seedlings,
implementation of a rural afforestation monitoring and
evaluation program, and training of higher-level foresters
and other forestry workers.
(c) integrated forestry, agriculture, and livestock development
in Gishwati comprising completion of investments and
maintenance of pastures and plantations developed in the
first project; development of an additional 2,000 ha of
protection plantations on degraded slopes; support for a land
use study and for new approaches to land management in the
Gishwati forest, and intensification of extension services
around the forest.
(d) maintenance of fuelwood and pole short rotation plantations
around Kigali and Butare developed in the first project.



- 14 -
(e) support for applied research (principally on-farm and on-
plantation trials) on animal nutrition, forest plantation
development, agroforestry and natural forest management; and
(f) support for a pilot pasture rehabilitation operation in the
Mutara region.
C. Geographical Coverage
3.04     The majority of the project's activities would be implemented in
the Nyungwe forest in southwest Rwanda and in the Gishwati forest in the
northwest. IBRD Map 20054. The Nyungwe forest straddles three
prefectures: Gikongoro, Cyangugu and a small part of KiLuye. The forest
has been divided into four zones, Unite Gestion de Zone (UGZ's) for which
protection activities are to be financed by IDA, CCCE, FED and Swiss Aid in
parallel. IBRD Map 90057. IDA-financed activities would be in UGZ3, a
25,000 ha. area southeast of the forest in Gikongoro prefecture in the
communes of Mudasomwa, Kivu and Nshili. The Gishwati forest is located in
Gisenyi prefecture. Pasture and plantation maintenance and development
would take place inside the legally designated area of the forest where
conversion of degraded forest has occurred and is planned while extension
activities would be concentrated in the communes surrounding the zorest,
namely Rwerere, Mutura and Kanama. Other project activities i.e.,
maintenance of fuelwood plantations would take place around Kigali and
Butare, the two largest cities while the small pilot pasture improvement
component would be carried out in Mutara in the north east of Rwanda.
Agro-forestry programs would be concentrated ir. the communes around Kigali
and Butare. However, support for monitoring and evaluation of rutal
afforestation would be national in scope.
D. Detailed Features
Natural Forest Protection and Managoment (US$5.5 million)
3.05     The technical aspects of natural forest protection and management
are summarized in Annex 3. Details can be found in the project's
Implementation Volume. The component would include principally the
protection, conservation and management of the Nyungwe and Gishwati
forests.
3.06     Protection of Nyungwe Forest.  PACZN provides the basis for the
protection of the Nyungwe forest. PACZN calls for setting aside 40% of the
area of Nyungwe (i.e., 39,000 ha) as a natural ecosystem reserve and
carrying out management activities on the remaining 60%. PACZN also calls
for the development of a management structure and an applied research
program. The most important management activities would be a forest
inventory and establishment of buffer plantations. Other activities could
include silvicultural operations, enrichment plantings, timber stand
improvement and production pLantations of native species. Their exact



- 15 -
nature would be determined and agreed upon by Government and the donors
after the forest inventory but would exclude commercial timber extraction
which would be environmentally unsound on the steep slopes of Nyungwe and
economically unjustified. The four donors (para. 3.04) would follow the
guidelines set out by PACZN and subsequent modifications to these
guidelines discussed and agreed upon by the donors and DGF from time to
time5 .
3.07     On this basis, the project would establish about 900 ha of buffer
plantations to serve as a "zone of control" between agricultural areas and
the natural forest. The plantations of exotics, e.g. Pinus patula C.
Lusitanica and A. melanoxylon and lndigenous species, e.g., Polysciars
fulva, would consist of a strip of up to 200 meters wide along a 45
kilometer forest boundary located in Mudasomwa, Kivu and Nshili communes.
The practice of taungya (i.e. growing of crops between trees in the
plantation) would be allowed in a carefully supervised manner to reduce
site preparation and weeding costs and to involve the local population in
project activities. Before detailed planning of the buffer plantations can
proceed, it will be necessary to establish a legal boundary to conform with
the current state of the forest, The final choice of the boundary line and
the extent to which buffer plantations should extend outside it would be
made by a commission consisting of representatives of DGF and local
officials. Constitution of the commission and demarcation of legal
boundaries for the buffer plantations would be a condition of credit
effectiveness. The project would finance the construction of an
implementation unit near Tangabo or Busenyi and two outstations at Kivu and
Nshili communes. About 65 km of roads would be established to provide
access to the presently inaccessible forest boundary. A system of forest
guards and guard posts would be supported.
3.08     The project would support an ecological inventory designed to
complete an iaformation base on the biological and ecological composition
of the Afro-montane forest in Nyungwe and to improve the capability of
Rwandese personnel and institutions to deal with natural resource
assessment surveys. The inventory would include vegetational surveys,
compilation of a faunal list and surveys of endangered animal species.
Other aspects would include analyses of forest ecosystems, studies of soil
formation, for.hst hydrology and forest vegetation dynamics. To ensure
consistency in approach, the inventory would be implemented in all four
sections of the forest supervised by the donors. It has been agreed by DGF
and the donors that proposed project-supported inventory activities outside
the IDA zone (UGZ3) vould complete and complement those being carried out
by the other donors.
3.09     The project would also support the establishment of a natural
resources monitoring and evaluation program for Nyungwe but with potential
for extension to Gishwati and other natural forests. Activities to be
monitored would include the effectiveness of protection activities and the
5/ Two of these donors, the Swiss and the FED have already begun work in
Nyungwe forest. They have each established about 2,000 ha of pine
plantations including a buffer zone, and the Swiss have carried out a
series of experiments and trials in UGZ1. The CCCE plans to begin its
protection and management activities in 1987. Further details are in
Annex 3.



- 16 -
incidence and the impact on the forest of the fzllowing: illegal wood
extraction, agricultural encroachment, livestock grazing, harvesting of
non-industrial wood products, mining, wildlife poaching and highway
construction. Areas of key concern would be the impact of the above on
species composiLion of the forest, loss of topsoil, soil fertility,
disruption of stream flow, total water yield, sediment load, impact on
wildlife populations, loss of biological diversity and impact on forest
dwelling populations. The specific design of the natural resources
monitoring and evaluation program and the ecological inventory will be
firmed up during project start-up activities in 1987. Terms of reference
for the studies are included in Annex 3. Start-up of the design studies is
a condition of board presentation. During negotiations assurances were
obtained on the satisfactory completion of the design studies by Februery
1, 1988 and prompt execution of the designs thercsfter.
3.10     Protection of the Gishwati forest would be along the lines
described for Nyungwe.   The project would finance 200 ha of buffer
plantations to protect the 5,000 ha largely undegraded reaerve6 . The
buffer plantat 'ons would be established over a distance of 9 km. of the
north and east boundaries of the reserve where clearing has taken place for
potato cultivation. Another 9 km of buffer plantation would be made up of
the 2,000 ho of sawlog plantations to be established on steep denuded
slopes in the forest. The project would upgrade 10 km of trail along the
northern border of the reserve and employ additional forest guards to
strengthen patrolling of the forest. It is expected that once the natural
resources monitoring and evaluation program for Nyungwe is established its
activities will extend to Gishwati. Monitoring and evaluation would focus
on the incidence and impact of potato cultivation, livestock grazing, and
other agricultural activity on loss of topsoil, soil fertility and
hydrology in the forest.
Agroforestry and Rural Afforestation (US$1.8 million)
3.11     The project would promote agroforestry and rural afforestation
through the strengthening of seedling production and distribution programs.
This would involve the upgrading of 60 sector (village-level) nurseries in
eight communes to serve as training grounds for farmers in agroforestry
techniques and for production and dissemination of a wide variety of
agroforestry species. Beginring in the third project year, and depending
on the availability of interested farmer or village groups, efforts would
be initiated to privatize these nurseries. The project would also support
private sector initiatives in seedling production focused primarily on the
deve.opment of collective private nurseries managed by farmer groups.
About 20 new groups per year producing about 5,000 plants each would be
supported. The plants would be destined primarily for their own use.
Other initiatives in seedling production involving schools, private farmers
or non-governmental organWzations (NGOs) could also be supported according
to demand. Funding would be provided to DGF to establish a seed storage
center to facilitate seedling distribution.
6/ The original area of Gishwati forest was 2R,000 ha consisting of an
8,000 ha. military domain, 5,000 ha reserve and 15,000 ha degraded
area. During the first phase project about 5,400 ha of the degraded
portion was converted to pastures and plantations. Additional areas
have been cleared by potato growers leaving about 21,900 ha of forest
Table 2.1.



- 17 -
3.12     The project would assist DGF to initiate direct cost recovery for
seedlings produced in the sector nurseries. About 50Z of the cost of
seedling production is currently recovered through communal labor
(umuganda). The project would initiate payments for plsnts by
beneficiaries not participatinig in umuganda at a rate equal to the unit
cost of production of the plants.  Those contributing labor would receive
an initial number of plants free after which they would have to pay for
additional plants at about a third of their cost. It is expected that if
this pilot effort succeeds in the 60 nurseries proposed for project
support, it would be extended to other nurseries country-wide.  Careful
monitoring of the progress, weaknesses and strengths of the component would
be an important objective of the monitoring and evaluation program for
rural afforestation (pa a. 3.13).
3.13     The project would strengthen the implementation and management of
forestry sector programs through the training of higher-level foresters
(para. 3.28) and in-service training of forest technicians. Local
administrative personnel, educators, extension agents and others carrying
out forestry-related work would also be trained through short workshops and
seminars.  A monitoring and evaluation program would be supported to
improve DGF's supervision, planning and management capacity. Several
important programs and policies would be monitored such as seedling
production and distribution, forestry extension, market developments for
wood and wood products. The wood pricing and marketing study proposed
under the project would help develop important indicators for monitoring.
During the project build-up phase, a Rwandese cadre would be supported for
three months to study similar programs in Bank-assisted foLestry projects
in othev East African countries. He would develop the detailed design for
the monitoring and evaluation program on his return to Rwanda.   Assurances
were obtained from GOR that the draft detailed design would be discussed
with IDA by February 1, 1988 after which it would be finalized and promptly
implemented.
Integrated Forestry, Agriculture and Livestock Develop,nent in Gishwati
(US$10.0 million)
3.14     Support for integrated forestry, agriculture and livestock
aevelopment in Gishwati would comprise five activLties: maintenance of
plantations developed in the first project, comapletion of investments and
maintenance of pasture and dairy activities developed in the first project,
a land use study of the Gishwati forest and support for new approaches to
land management; development of new industrial protection plantaticns; and
intensification of extension services around the forest.
3.15     Maintenance of Indust:ial Plantations developed in the First
Proiect (US$0.3 million). The project would support the maintenance of
about 2,100 ha of industrial (largely pine) plantations established in
Phase I including upkeep of 50 km of plantation roads.  Maintenance
activities would inc',ude weeding, low pruning, some thinning and
supervision of tau..gya cultivators. The practice of taungya would be
permitted, as in Nyungwe, to reduce weeding costs and allow an additional
source of income for the local people. . rotation of different crops which
cause less disturbance to the soil, such as maize and peas, would bf



- 18 -
encouraged until plantations reach at least 18 months after which potato
cultivation (popular in the area) may be permitted. The taungya system
uvuld be rigorously supervised by pro4 act staff. A contrac~t would be drawn
up between each participant and the Gishwati subunit stating clearly the
obligations of the cultivator and penalties for non-compliance. During
negotiations assurances were obtained from GOR that the contracts would be
rigorously enforced.
3.16     Completion and Maintenance of Pasture and Dairy Activities
developed in the first prolect (US$2.8 million). The first project
developed 3,400 ha of pastures and some revenue-earning entities namely a
dairy, livestock technical support centre (CAT), a beef fattening facility
(Zone d'embouche), and a veterinary pharmacy. The proposed project would
finance the maintenance of about 1,600 ha of pastures for a period of two
years or until milk production reaches a break-even level of 30 liters per
day whichever comes earlier. Funding would be provided for pasture upkeep,
and administrative costs of the Gishwati livestock unit. The project would
finance the establishment of 30 ha of quarantine zones, a slaughter slab at
the beef fattening facility, and the purchase of dairy equipment to make
the dairy functional at 5,000 litres per day processing capacity.
Additional funding would be included to bring dairy processing capacity up
to 10,00o liters per day but use of these funds would be condit'oned on
joint IDA-GOR agreement on dairy processing recommendations of a livestock
subsector study financed under SPPF (para. 3.20). The project would
introduce the use of artificial insemination to optimize the number of
cattle on the pastures and reduce the Dossibility of disease. The purchase
of pure breed cattle from Kivu would nevertheless continue to complete
charging of Phase I pastures (including those for CAT). This would be
financed through loca, commercial banks. Agreement has been reached that
as of February 1, 1987 credit would no longer be extended to pasture owners
at subsidized rates for cattle purchase.  The Gishwati project unit would
assist groups of livestock owners in an equitable manner to prepare the
necessary papers for loans. Assurances were obtained to this effect.
During the course of project preparation, a number of agreements were
reached on the technical aspects of pasture and dairy developments. These
are summarized in para 7.0).
3.17     The project would assist GOI to create a Federation of Livestock
Owners (FDLO) to take over management of the pasture activities including
the revenue earning entities. This is in line with the objective of
pri"atizing management of the Gishwati pasture activities and gradually
diminishing the role of the GBK project unit (para 4.11). FDLO would be
constituted with the help of local and foreign technical assistants during
the first three years of the project and is expected to assume its
management role by end ;989. Federation members and officials wculd
receive training and study tours to develop their managerial capacities.
Pending the formation of the Federation, ranagement of pasture activities
would be decentralized from Kigali to Gishwati. The revenue-earning
entities would be run as commercial enterprises from the beginning of the
project.
3.18     Support for New A  roaches to Land Management and a Land Use Study
_S$1.8 million). The project would include a sum of US$1.8 million to
implement new approaches to land management in the degraded areas of the
Gishwati forest based on the recommendations of a land use and management



- 19 -
study which would be carried out starting during the project build-up
phase. The study (estimated cost US$0.3 million) would explore new and
more cost-effective approaches to management of land resources in the
Gishwati forest, particularly the remaining, 9,000 ha degraded area
scheduled for conversion. A further important objective of the study would
be to address environmental concerns stemming from increased livestock and
human encroachment, in particular in the 5,000 ha undegraded reserve.
Environmental problems are complicated by the premature clearing by potato
growers of a large part (Lp to 3,000 ha.) of the degraded forest area (some
of this on steep slopes). The land use study would be carried out in two
phases. In the first phase (about 6 months) the study would:
(a) determine currer. land uses; and (b) evaluate the relative suitability
of the study area for various possible land uses and identify sites
suitable for development of forest protection plantations and other types
of development. In the next phase (about 6 months), the study would (a)
explore how to involve the local people more closely in the development of
the area; (bhl select the most suitable combination of land uses for the
study area which, in conjunction with forest protection measures, would
also provide solutions for destocking of cattle and (c) produce a detailed
management plan including investment proposals for future land/livestock
development. Terms of reference are attached in Annex 5.
3.19     Completion of the study would take a maximum of 12 months.  It
would be carried out by a team of technical assistants in collaboration
with the Rwandese beginning February 1987. About USSl1S million (of which
US$1.05 million IDA-financed) would be used to implement new approaches to
land development including the possibility for pastu.-e establishment by
farmers (rather than by the Gishwati unit) in combination with some
cropping and agroforestry activities. A small amount of the funds
(US$35,000 IDA-financed) would be used at the beginning of the project to
increase supervision of potato growers and to intro. -ce alternative
techniques of cultivation and other crops (e.g. peas, beans and maize) that
are more ecologically beneficial. About US$300,000 (of which US$210,000
IDA-financed) would be used for a limited amount of pasture establishment
in 1988 to absorb pure-breed cattle that will become available from Zaire
and to experiment with increasee levels of farmer participation in pasture
establishment. The remaining funds US$1.2 million (of which US$800,000
IDA-financed) would be used to continue land development after GOR and IDA
have reached agreement on an action plan for future land use in Gishwati
based on the recommendation of the land use study. Disbursement of the
US$800,000 in IDA funds woulc be conditioned on GOR-IDA agreement on the
action plan.
3.20     A livestock sub-sector study financed under SPPF and scheduled for
completion by December 31, 1987 would provide complementary information on
appropriate types of dairy processing, pricing of dairy products, marketing
and distribution channels for milk production in Gishwati and elsewhere in
Rwanda. This would address problems of processing, pricing and marketing
arising out of the first project. The results of this study would also be
an important element in the consideration of future land use for livestock
cevelopment in Gishwati,
3.21     SUPrt for New Sswlog Plantations (US$1.7 million).  The project
would finance the establishment of 2,000 ha of sawlog plantations to
protect denuded forest areas where there is environmental damage caused by



- 20 -
the excessive forest clearing and poorly implemented potato cultivation.
Plantations would be established principally on areas vith average slopes
over 400. The plantations would also complete the buffer zone to be
developed as protection for the 5,000 ha forest reserve (para. 3.10).
Specific sites would be identified during the first phase of the land use
study (para. 3.18). The plantations would be established over a four-year
period and would comprise principally Cypressus lusitanica and Acacia
melanoxylor with cypress on the better site classes. About 40 km. of new
plantation roads would be constructed.
3.22     Intensification of Extension Activities.  (US$3.4 million) After
many years of uncoordinated extension activity, Government is currently in
the process of establishing a national extension policy and working out an
extension structure. As part of the process a national extension seminar
was held in February 1987 in which IDA staff participated as observers.
Ideas emerging out of the seminar indicate an extension policy and
structure along the lines being pursued in the IDA-financed
Gitarama/Minagri Project (Credit 1669 RW). Proposed project extension
activities would be carried out within the framework of the latter project
but the extension structure proposed should be sufficiently flexible to
allow for fit into the national structure when this is finally articulated.
The project would continue and intensify the delivery of extension services
begun in the first phase in the communes of Rwerere, Kanama and Mutura on
the northwest border of the Gishwati forest. Support would also be
provided to improve coordination of extension activities in the Gisenyi
prefecture and to initiate the streamlining of extension ser.ice delivery
along the lines being pursued in the Gitarama/Minagri Project.
3.23     The project would support the extension of already proven and
successful themes and packages focusing on stallfeeding of cattle and
goats; use of composting and farm-yard manure; erosion control through on-
farm terracing; improved techniques for potato cultivation including use of
fertilizer and insecticides; and development of woodlots. Increased
communitl participation in forest nurseries through contribution of labor
and direct cost recovery for nursery plants would be introduced. Support
would be provided for the development of new themes and packages focusing
on on-farm testinig and introduction of multi-purpose woody species known to
be beneficial for erosion control, fertilization, fodder and fuelwood;
development of better terracing techniques for erosion control and soil
conservation; optimum methods of integration of livestock on small farms
without sufficient space for fodder production and limited fallow land.
Other areas would be the introduction of themes on the value of the natural
forest and improved animal nutrition and health care.
3.24     The project would support in-country training of extension agents
and subject matter specialists. The current system of one extension agent
and one "aide veterinaire" per sector (group of ten villages) will be
gradually replaced by a system of one extension agent per sector assisted
by three sublec'. matter specialists at the commune level. The project
would provide vehicles and equipment to make the extension agents and
specialists more effective in this work. Agreement has been reached that
payment for the vehicles wouild be deducted monthly from the salaries of the
personnel at a rate to be determined by Minagri. The project would promote
the privatization of input supply and delivery through groups of farmers
and/or private merchants who would be encouraged and ass4sted to take over



- 21 -
distribution of certain inputs. At the prefecture level, the project would
support the development of a core unit of three AOs under the "prefecture
agronome," to coordinate extension activities, and to assist with the
development of new themes and packages. The unit would also assist in the
training of extension agents dna in -onitoring and evaluation of extension
activities over the prefecture. The core unit could later move to the
sous-prefecture level if it is so decided under the emerging national
extension structure. Changes in the project's proposed extension
organization should be discussed and agreed with IDA by December 31, 1989.
Assurances were obtained from GOR to this effect. Extension service
delivery, currently the responsibility of several donor-financed projects,
would gradually come under the supervision of the core unit.
Mainrtenance of Kigali-Butare Fuelwood and Pole Short Rotation Plantations
(US$2.0 million)
3.25     About 7,000 ha of plantations were established in Phase I around
the major towns of Kigali and Butare to supply fuelwood (for charcoal) and
building poles to the urban population. About 2,000 ha of these
plantations are not expected to be productive due to poor sites but will be
important for protection purposes (para. 1.16). The project would support
the maintenance of about 3,600 ha of the productive plantations,
maintenance of 700 km of firebreaks, 60 km of access tracks, and
construction of 32 km of extraction tracks for harvesting of the older
plantations. In carrying out maintenance operations overheads would be
kept to the minimum possible level. In this regard, only stands four years
and younger (planted after 1983) would be weeded and only those which on
average have a stocking greater than 40% (640 stems per ha). Tl'e project
would finance labor, small tools and a tractor trailer for extrac.ion
activities.
Applied Research (US$0.3 million)
3.26     Applied research would involve mainly on-farm and on-plantation
t.rials designed to respond to problems directly related to project
implementation. Support would be provided for on-site experim.ents and teed
trials on the Kikuyu grass pastures of Gishwati and the use of concentrate
feeds and minerals. Adaptive research on the intensification of stall fed
animals would be carried out in close collaboration with the extension
services.  This would be closely linked to agroforestry research trials_on
the use of tree and shrub fodder in animal husbandry. Other agro-forestry
trials would involve on-farm selection of leguminous trees that contribute
to soil fertility, the impact of various tree management systems such as
pruning and pollarding on crop yields, and studies of the interaction
between trees and farm-crops. In relation to fuelwood, a particularly
important research area would be close spacing trials of fast growing
species. Applied research on forest plantations would include monitoring
of some permanent sample plots in project plantations to help answer
fundamental questions on choice of species and provenances for specific
locations. Other support would be provided for analysis of existing data
on sample plots, establishment of f rtilizer trials, and establishment of
species tests for new plantation areas in Gishwati and Nyungwe and on
methods for protection against rat damage of cypress plantations. As
information develops from the Nyungwe ecological inventory ISAR would
assist with natural forest management research (para. 4.12).



- 22 -
Mutara Pasture Rehabilitation (US$0.5 million)
3.27     The project would fund a pilot experimental effort to rehabilitate
pastures developed under the IDA-financed Mutara II Rural Development
project (Credit 937 RW) closed December 31, 1986. Although project
implementation has been satisfactory, the sustainability of the project's
impact is in doubt because of the continuous deterioration of pastures
resulting from the beneficiaries' inability to eradicate noxious shrub and
grass species. Recent consultant reports proposed bush clearing, ploughing
and pasture re-seeding by tractors on the most degraded parts covering
about 10,000 ha or a quarter of total pasture area. The efficacy of the
technique proposed has, however, not been proven in the project area's
conditions. To overcome the aJove problems, the proposed project would
support experimentation with rehabilitation of 1,500 ha of pastures over a
two year period. The experimentation would test the technical, financial
and economic feasibility of the pasture rehabilitation methods proposed in
the consultants' report and compare this with pasture rehabilitation mainly
through labor intensive methods presently being tried. The project would
finance some equipment and labor costs. GOR and IDA would review results
of the pilot operation at the end of two years and draw conclusions on
whether to extend rehabilitation by this method to the rest of the Mutara
region. During negotiations, the methodology for evaluating results from
pasture rehabilitation was discussed and agreed.
Technical Assistance and Training
3.28     Provision has been made for inclusion of technical assistance and
training in several components. A summary of these requirements is
included in Annex 2. The project would finance a total of 10.5 manyears of
long-term technical assistance and 72 man-months of short term
consultancies for project implementation and management. Key long-term
positions would be five years of an ecologist and one and a half years of a
financial management expert for the Nyungwe component and three years of a
zootechnician for the Gishwati pasture component. About 43 man-months of
consultancies would be for initiation of activities and studies during the
project build-up phase. It is expected that about 15 man-months of the
short-term consultancies could be provided by locally available experts.
Assurances were obtained that the terms of reference and conditions of
employment of technical assistants would be acceptable to IDA. The project
would finance 23 man-years of regional and overseas training and study
tours. The bulk of this (18 manyears) would be for graduate training of
about eight higher level foresters. Candidates must have a minimum of a
bachelor's degree in forestry or agronomy and must sign a bQnd of service
to GOR on completion of training for a length of time to be decided by GOR
but in no case less than twice the period spent in training. Assurances
were obtained to this effect. During negotiations GOR and IDA agreed on
criteria for selection of candidates.

E. Proiect Costs
3.29     Total project cost (including direct taxes of about US$1.0
million)7 is estimated at US$20.1 million (RF 1,660.2 million), of which
US$8.5 million or 42% is foreign exchange. Costs are based on quantity and
unit price estimates prepared by DGF, the GBK project unit, ISAR and other
Government agencies. Unit price estimates for vehicles, equipment and
civil works are in line with past project experience and recent civil works
contracts.
3.30     Price contingencies amount to 12% of total base costs plus
physical contingencies and have been calculated on the basis of foreign
inflation rates of 3% in 1987, 1% in 1988-90, and 3.5% in 1991-92 and local
inflation rates of 3% in 1987, 6% in 1988, 5% in 1989 and 3% in 1990-92.
Prices are as of mid-1986, updated to May 1987. Physical contingencies of
102 have been included for selected project items such as civil works and
building construction where there are sources of uncertainty. Expatriate
consultant costs have been estimated on the basis of US$10,000 per man-
month (inclusive of salaries, fees and travel). Project costs are
summarized in Table 3.1. Details are presented in Annex 7 and in the
Implementation Volume.
F. Financing
3.31     A financing plan by major project components is presented in Table
3.2. The proposed IDA Credit of US$14.1 million would cover 70% of project
costs and would be provided to GOR on standard terms and condit'ons. The IDA
Credit would finance the following: all technical assistance co;ts and the
costs of overseas and regional training and studies, 70% of the cost of civil
works (consisting mainly of land preparation for plantations, road construction
and pasture maintenance), equipment and vehicles, and 60% of operating costs
excluding salaries and per diems. IDA financing includes a PPF, refinanced
under the credit, of US$1.5 million for build-up of project activities. GOR
would provide US$6.0 million or 30% of project costs including taxes. GOR's
contribution would finance all staff salaries and per diems, 30% of civil works
costs and 40% of operating costs other than salaries and per diem.
7/ Import duties have been excluded since the Government has indicated
that these would be waived.

G. Procurement
3.32     Project procurement would be managed through three main implementation
units: the GBK project unit, DGF, and Nyungwe project unit. Prior IDA review
of project procurement procedures (draft tender documents, bid evaluation,
award and contract signature) would be required for all civil works contracts
and all equipment contracts estimatee to cost over US$50,000. It is estimated
that about 70% of the contract's vaJ3ie would be subject to prior review. Given
the small size of the various civil works contrvcts, they are unlikely to
attract an intecnational contractor. For this reason, all civil works (US$6.20
million) would be expected to be procured following LCB procedures acceptable
to IDA, although international contractors would not be excluded. Contracts
for vehicles and equipment (total cost US$2.80 million) estimated to cost over
US$100,000 would be procured through International Competitive Bidding
following the Bank's guidelines. All equipment contracts estimated to cost
less than US$50,000 (an aggregate not to exceed US$0.6 million) would be
procured through local shopping with price quotations from at least three
suppliers. All IDA-financed expatriate staff would be internationally
recruited following procedures acceptable to IDA. IDA financed training
feli-cwships (estimated to cost US$0.9 million) would be awarded following
procedures acceptable to IDA. Operating cost expenditures, e.g., spare parts,

IV. PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.01     The project would be implemented over a six-year period comprising
a one year build-up phase (1987) financed by PPF and a five-year
implementation phase (1988-92). Work during the build-up phase would
include principally the completion of a wood pricing and marketing study
(para. 2.04) a study and design of the ecological inventory and natural
resources monitoring and evaluation program (para. 3.09), and a land use
and management study for the Gishwati forest (para. 3.18). Other
activities would include priority maintenance for Phase I plantations, the
installation of urgently needed equipment to improve dairy processing
capacity in Gishwati, construction of a slaughter slab at the cattle



- 29 -
fatteniing facility and increased supervision of potato growers. A project
implementation schedule is included in Annex 1. About 31 incremental
higher-level staff would be required. They would be recruited locally. A
staffing schedule is presented in Annex 2.
A. Project Management
4.02     The Ministry of Agriculture, Livestock and Forests and within it,
thc office of the Secretary General, would be responsible for overall
project implementation and management. A Project Management Committee
(PMC) chaired by the Secretary General with representation from the
Ministries of Finance and Plan and including the Directors-General of
Livestock, Forests, Directors of Agricultural Promotion, Project Control
and Evaluation, and ISAR, the GBK project coordinator, and the head of the
Nyungwe implementat.on unit would be responsible for project coordination
and supervision. Prior to negotiations GOR established the PMC by
ministerial decision. The terms of reference of the PMC were agreed with
IDA during negotiations. The managers of all project components would
report directly to the PMC which would also be responsible for reviewing
and approving annual work plans, budgets, and monitoring project progress
through periodic meetings. The PMC would delegate day-to-day project
implementation to three main agencies or units, DGF, the GBK and Nyungwe
units (Chart 1). Since a key project objective is to better integrate
project implementation into the ministry structure, it is expected that by
the end of the project, all activities managed by temporary units such as
GBK and Nyungwe would be fully integrated into the normal structure of the
ministry or transferred to private sector management (Chart 2).
4.03     The NyunKwe Proiect Unit (UGZ3) would be responsible for
implementing the Nyuingwe natural forest protection component (Chart 3). It
would liaise with local extension parsonnel to "sensitize" people on the
value of the natural forest. The main unit would be headed by an AO
project manager responsible for overall implementation. The subunits would
be manned by A2 foresters. Supervision of field activities at each
location would be the responsibility of three A3 foresters.
4.04     Implementation of the ecological inventory and natural resources
monitoring and evaluation program would be the responsibility of an
ecologist assisted by a socio-economist and 16 months of short-term
consultancies in various fields of expertise. The ecologist would have an
AO Rwandese forester as a counterpart and the assistance of three A2
foresters and a team of field staff. ISAR would provide guidance on
research aspects of the inventory and the monitoring and evaluation program
(para. 4.12). Some facilities for visiting researchers would be provided
at UGZ3. DGF would have oversight responsibility for the technical quality
of activities undertaken at UGZ3. This would be achieved through the PMC
and through on site visits of DGF's Director or his designate. The unit
woule maintain close contact with the Conservation Center through donor
coordination meetings. The Conservation Center is to be established by GOR
(with CCCE financing) mid-1988. The center will coordinate donor
activities and scientific research programs (with ISAR) and train forest
guards. Terms of reference for its operation should be satisfactory to
IDA. Assurances were obtained to this effect. Assurances were also

4.05     DGF would implement the agroforestry and rural afforestation

investment and operating budgets by component, showing the human,

indicates the source of funds for items in the investment and operating

and (vi) procurement plans for purchases a..d works scheduled in the annual
work program, including type of bidding and timetables. The draft work
