V. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES AND FINANCIAL ASPECTS
A. Production
5.01     Although a major thrust of the project would be in activities
where direct production benefits are difficult to quantify, a significant
number of activities would yield readily measurable outputs. The project
would result in the production of an incremental 1.2 million m3 of
industrial wood and sawlogs from the Gishwati plantations. Maintenance of
the Kigali-Butare fuelwood plantations would result in an incremental 0.8
million m3 of wood, representing about 10% of those cities' charcoal
requirements by 1999. The project would increase fuelwood production



- 34 -
through its farm forestry activities. As a result of seedling production
and promotion of agroforestry through training programs, it is estimated
that an incremental 0.4 million m3 of fuelwood could be produced over a 25-
year development period.
5.02     An incremental 59 million liters of milk and 3,390 tons of meat
would result from the livestock activities. Some export of meat to
regional markets is being explored and could earn additional foreign
exchange (para 5.05). Intensification of extension activities is expected
to result in th4 additional production of food crops, mainly maize, peas
and potatoes (Annex 9). Most of the additional production would be
marketed bringing in extra income.
B. Marketing
5.03     Incremental project production from the fuelwood and pole short
rotation plantations would find a ready market. Most of this wood is
destined to be converted to charcoal to supply the towns of Kigali and
Butare. The rest is for use as construction poles also in the towns.
Fuelwood produced through farm forestry activities is expected to be used
primarily for home consumption by the farm households, although some amount
may be sold for charcoal production as the rural wood market develops.
,.04     The market for industrial wood may present a problem.  There is
very little information on this market, but it appears that there is a low
preference for pine which makes up the majority of industrial wood
currently planted in Rwanda including wood from the first phase Gishwati
plantations. Demand for industrial wood estimated at 28,000 m3 in 1981 is
far below its potential. GOR believes that the potential can be tapped and
is working with Swiss assistance to develop projects that would utilize
pine. The proposed project supports a wood pricing anc marketing study
(para.2.04) to explore potential avenues for commercialization of pine and
to provide more information on the market for industrial wood. Proposed
second phase plantations are for protection of denuded slopes in the
Gishwati forest (para. 3.22). The plantations would consist of preferred
species such as acacia melanoxylon and cypressus lusitanica for which the
market is prepared to pay a premium thus providing the option of future
marketing of the wood.
5.05     The current market for meat and dairy products is largely in the
urban areas. Incremental meat and milk p >r4uction would be marketed mostly
in Kigali where project milk output will most likely substitute for
imported powdered milk. Distribution and marketing of dairy products in
Rwanda is underdeveloped, and there are several bottlenecks along the
production distribution chain. Processing facilities for dairy products
are generally small in size and some like Gishwati have operated
inefficiently. Transportation is a problem ard distribution channels are
few. Partlv as a result of this, but also because of a high administered
producer price of milk, the price to the consumer at $0.8 to $1.0 per liter
is very high.  The market appears to absorb all available output at this
price at the present time, but the capacity to absorb future quantities of
output at current prices is an issue. In the course of project preparation
agreement was reached with GOR to review the milk pricing mechanism and
adjust producer prices in line with demand.   As a condition of credit
effectiveness, GOR will develop a iricing mechanism for milk acceptalle to



- 35 -
IDA. The objective of the pricing mechanism will be to encourage efficient
production and marketing of milk and other d&iiry products. Decisions on a
pricing mechanism would be based on the findings of the livestock subsector
study financed under SPPF (para. 3.20) which will review dairy pricing,
processing and marketing problems in depth and make recommendations for
future policy. The study is scheduled for completion by December 31, 1987.
The study would also review meat marketing and pricing in relation to dairy
products. GOR is currently in the process of negotiating meat export
contracts with neighboring countries. This, coupled with the reduction in
illegal meat imports trom Uganda is expected to open up new channels in the
meat market.
C. erices
5.06     Project financial and econoinic prices are sunmmarized in Table 5.1.
Estimation of these prices, some of which, like fuelwood, are for goods not
traded internationally, are explained in detail below by product. The
price of fuelwood (used primarily for charcoal), from state and communal
plantations is set by a min_.sterial decree of February 1983 at RF 500 per
stere or RF 800 per m3 solid. The market (stumpage) priee for fuelwood
ranges from RF 0-500/m3 in areas far away from urban concentrations. Near
the urban areas such as Kigali and Butare the stumpage price is between RF
500-1,000/m3. Based on the average prices near these two towns, the
financial price of fuelwood from the Kigali-Butare plantations has been
taken at RF 750/m3. The economic price of fuelwood is based on the assumed
response of urban dwellers to a situation in which charcoal from the
plantations was no longer available. From the various alternative energy
sources available, the burning of briquets has been taken as the "base
case" alternative to charcoal. Other sources of energy would be gas,
electricity, and kerosene. However, el.ctricity and gas are obtainable
only at very high cost and household preferences in Rwanda appear to be
markedly against use of kerosene. Use of the replacement cost of the GBK
plantations as the basis for the economic price would have resulted in a
price of RF 2,700 per m3 solid. The GBK plantations are however probably
unrepresentative of costs. Farm forestry is another potential source of
supply for urban charcoal. In the absence of new fuelwood plantations and
given the reduction in the supply of free wood from the rapidly
disappearing savannah regions, an increase in farm forestry activity would
be likely in light of the scarcity of, and high prices for charcoal.
However, this source of fuelwood supply for charcoal making would, in the
short to medium-term, be both insufficient and unreliable to fulfill urban
needs because increases in supply would go primarily towards mak-ng up for
existing deficits in the rural areas. In the longer term, however, farm
forestry could be expected to contribute significantly to fulfilling urban
needs and as it becomes a more important source of supply for the urban
areas evaluation of the economic price of fuelwood would be more
appropriately based on the replacement cost of wood produced through farm
forestry. It is expected that future fuelwood projects will use this mode
of production as the relevant yardstick for the economic cost of fuelwood.
In the meantime, for this project, use of papyrus briquets, the manufacture
of which is being developed in Rwanda, has beer. assumed to be the mos
plausible alternative to plantation based charcoal. On this basis, the
economic price of fuelwood (converted to charcoal) is estimated at RF 1,835
per m3 solid. This is arrived at by comparing the cost, thermal efficiency
and calorific value of briquets to that of charcoal for producing a given
amount of energy. Details of the calculation are provided in Annex 9.

5.07     Industrial Wood.  The present market price of sawnwood is on
average RF 3,000 per m3 standing and poles RF 4,000 per m3 standing. This
compares with an "official" price of approximately RF 5,700 and RF 4,700
per m3 solid for sawnwood and poles, respectively.
5.08     The financial price of poles has been taken at its market value
while that of sawnwood has been adjusted to reflect a possible decrease in
its real value over the medium term (para. 5.04) For the economic
analysis, market prices for the two categories of industrial wood have been
adjusted to reflect a 2% real price decrease per year from 1987-96. After
that, present Government efforts to expand demand, coupled with a slowdown
in the present rate of supply expansion, is assumed to cause prices to
stabilize.
5.09     The producer price of milk is fixed by the Government.  The basis
on which this price is determined is unclear. The present farm-gate price
is fixed at between RF 40 to 45 per liter and results in a retail price of
between RF 65 and 80 per liter, depending on location. The present
producer price has been retained for the financial analysis but adjusted
downwards by RF 10 for farmers producing by more traditional methods in the
communes outside the Gishwati forest (para. 5.13). For the purpose of the
economic analysis, the price of milk has been based on the import parity
price of powdered milk. Imported milk in powdered form is currently a
significant source of supply, and in the absence of the Gishwati project
would remain so. The import parity price is estimated at RF 24 per liter.
Details are presented in Annex 9.



- 37 -
5.10     The price of meat is also set by the Government.  The present
farmgate price is fixed at an average of RF 55 per kg., which is low
relative to the price of milk. This probably reflects the depressing
effect brought about by illegal imports from Uganda during the time of its
recent troubles (para. 5.05). Given that this source of supply is
diminishing and that GOR is negotiating meat export contracts with
neighboring countries, the economic price of meat has been taken at its
export parity value estimated at RF 93 per kg (live weight). Details are
provided in Annex 9. Details of input prices are also given in Annex 9.
D. Financial Analysis of Revenue-Earning Entities
5.11     Financial projections (income statement, cash flow, balance sheet)
have been prepared for the revenue earning entities in Gishwati namely the
dairy, technical support center (CAT), the beef fattening facility (zone
d'embouche), and the veterinary pharmacy. These projections together with
assumptions are in knnex 8. Due to improper management and a heavy
financial burden in the first phase which has affected the financial
viability of these entities, it was considered that the entities would be
unable to repay GOR their full investment cost as a long term debt. About
60% of the cost on average was therefore considered as equity contribution
from the Government specifically, RF 53.9 million for the dairy, FRW 11.0
million for the beef fattening facility RF 2.77 million for the veterinary
pharmacy and FRW 50.0 million for the CAT. The balance between the full
establishment cost and the equity contribution has been considered a long
term debt bearing a 7.92% annual interest rate, a grace period of five
years and a 20-year repayment period including the five-year grace period.
Unpaid interest during the grace period was calculated for each year and
added to the principal and to gross fixed assets. Each of the above
entities would in the future keep separate accounts in a corporate manner.
By mid 1989 the government should make proposals to IDA, concerning the
specific modalities for the management structure and ownership of the
dairy, CAT, zone d'embouche and veterinary pharmacy under the proposed
Federation of Livestock Owners. The entities would reimburse the
Government in Rwandese francs. The foreign exchange risk and interest
rate risk would be borne by the entities. Assurances were obtained that
the onlending arrangement would apply to the proposed project and be
retroactive to the previous one.
E. Farm level Financial Impact
5.12     A 30 ha pasture model was developed to look at the financial
impact of the livestock activities on an average pasture block in Gishwati.
Details are presented in Annex 8. The financial analysis is based on the
assumption that pasture owners will bear the full cost of investments made
in Phase 2 but only 50% of Phase I investment costs. Poor management in
phase I resulting in higher than anticipated pasture establishment costs
makes it difficult to fully recover these costs para (5.15). Based on the
above, the financial rate of return to an average pasture over a 20-year
period is estimated at 65%   By sub-project full development (year 9) net



- 38 -
revenues on the pasture would equal about RF 492,000. With about 15
farmers per pasture, an average farmer would experience a net income
increase of about RF 33,000 representing about a 60% per annum increase in
family income. Sensitivity analysis was carried out to see the impact on
returns of a decrease in the producer price of milk. The analysis shows
that the financial rate of return (FRR) would remain satisfactory at 51%
and 36% respectively, should the milk price be decreased by 10 or 20%. The
FRR would fall to 17% with a 30% decrease in price. These results depend
on the availability of pure treed cattle and crosses. The project would
assist farmers to obtain commercial bank loans to purchase the cattle and
would introduce artificial insemination to optimize pasture use. (para.
3.17)
5.13     Three indicative farm models were developed to look at the
financial impact of the project's extension activities. Estimates in the
models are necessarily tentative since they involve a measure of
experimentation with new practices, e.g., introduction to multipurpose
woody species. The models are summarized in Table 5.2. Details are
presented in Annex 9.

F. Cost Recovery arid Fiscal Impact
5.14     The project would have a positive fiscal impact on the
Government's budget through: (a) cost recovery from the Gishwati pastures
(b) some cost recovery from the Gishwati revenue-earning entities; and (c)
sales of wood from the forest plantations. A modest amount of revenues
would also be generated from sales of seedlings in the agroforestry
nurseries. Details of projected Government cash flow are contained in
Annex 8. Government cash flow would be negative in the first four years
of the project. Major outflows during these years would consist of the
costs of the project, which together with debt service charges would reach
RF 204 million by year 6. In the ensuing years recurrent cost
expenditures to sustain project activities would amount to an average RF
90 million per year. These recurrent costs would be balanced against
revenues of an average RF 310 million per year from all the sources above,
thus resulting in a positive net cash flow.
5.15     Cost recovery from the Gishwati pastures has been reviewed in
some detail. The cost of establishing the 3,340 ha. of pastures under the
Phase 1 project amounts to RF 767.0 million (Annex 8) up to the end of
1986. Rapid weed infestation, poor management and use of high cost
materials resulted in higher than anticipated pasture establishment costs
in phase I. These costs cannot be fully recovered from the pasture owners
without jeopardizing the financial viability of the pastures. As a result
50% of the pasture establishment costs from the first project will be
considered a subsidy. The remaining 50% will be recovered on the
following terms: a repayment period of 20 years including a grace period
of 5 years and an interest rate of 7.92%. This corresponds to a yearly
repayment of RF 44.4 million for 3,340 ha or RF 13,200 per ha per year.
This amount of recovery corresponds to approximately 30'* of a pasture's
revenues which is considered a reasonable level of cost recovery. The
five-year grace period originally included in the rental agreement signed
between the beneficiaries of the pastures and MINAGRI has been kept since
pastures require about this long to constitute their herd. It is
estimated that another RF 120.0 million will be spent between January 1,
1987, and December 31, 1989, to complete the establishment of Phase I
pastures in Gishwati. This will be fully recovered on the same terms as
above, resulting in a yearly repayment of RF 14.0 million for 3,340 ha or
RF 4,200 per hectare per year. Since pasture beneficiaries will share the
operating costs of the Federation of Livestock Owners in early 1990, it is
not expected that the Government will continue to finance any costs of the
Gishwati livestock component as of December 31, 1989. The level of cost
recovery proposed here represents a five-fold increase over that proposed
by MINAGRI in the existing rental contract. Agreement has been reached
with GOR on the new cost recovery levels and on the revision of the old
rental contract. During negotiations, GOR and IDA agreed on the substance
of the revision to the rental contract and on the phasing of its
execution. The proposed cost recovery would apply to investments made in
the first project as well as those proposed in the second project.
Assurances were obtained to this effect during negotiations. As a
condition of credit effectiveness GOR would, as a first step, prepare a
modified rental contract acceptable to IDA and, as a second step, present
evidence satisfactory to IDA that pasture beneficiaries on already
established pastures have si2ned the rental contract.



- 40 -
5.16     Sales of wood from the plantations would represent a significant
contribution to Government revenues. Beginning in 1991, about RF 85
million would be realized annually on average from the Kigali-Butare
plantations. By the year 2000, contributions from wood sales in both the
fuelwood and industrial plarntations would reach an estimated RF 400
million per annum. It is proposed that the annual yield from each
plantation be sold standing (stumpage) to the highest bidder, rather than
at some fixed statutory stumpage rate which would be the same for all
plantations irrespective of their accessibility and which may 'Aso tend to
lag behind tne development of the free-market price. Only if the sale of
the standing wood does not attract sufficient bids, e.g. if entrepreneurs
generally do not have the means of doing the logging, should DGF consider
doing this operation itself and then sell the wood at roadside in the
plantations.
VI. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Justification
6.01     The project is justified by:
(a) the econ3my-wide ecological and hydrological benefits stemming
from the protection and conservation of two of Rwanda's
remaining tropical high altitude natural forests;
(b) institutional development benefits arising from improved
planning, monitoring and evaluation of forestry sector
programs.
(c) the production of an incremental 2.4 million m3 of fuelwood and
industrial wood valued at $45.3 million and the incremental
production of 2.3 million liters of milk and 136 tons of meat
per annum at full development valued at $0.55 million and 0.13
million, respectively;
(d) the employment generation of about 2,150 man-years per annum
during the project investment period; and
(e) the wide distribution of benefits covering over 75,000 farm
families, most of them in absolute poverty.
6.02     The major project benefits would stem from protection and
conservation of two of Rwanda's remaining natural forests of the Zaire-Nile
Divide, the Nyungwe and Gishwati forests. Quantification of the benefits of
natural forest protection and conservation is often difficult due to
methodological and data problems. In Rwanda's case this is exacerbated by
the paucity of "hard" information on the ecological impact of the afro-
montane forests. It is, however, generally accepted that Rwanda's forests
play an important biological, hydrological and ecological role, which in the
absence of protection would result in adverse environmental and economic
consequences. The Bank's recent OPN 11.2 on wild lands management lists
Rwarda's Afro-montane forests as one of the wild lands of special concern



- 41 -
internationally recognized to be "exceptionally important in conserving
biological diversity or perpetuating environmental services". Rwanda's
Afro-montane forests play a very important regulatory role for the country's
river system. The Nyungwe forest, in particular, is a major catchment area
for the Nyabarongo River system of the region and the nation as a whole.
The eastern side of Nyungwe consists of a series of water catchments which
eventually feed into the Akagera basin for which an important hydroelectric
project has been designed. The remaining undegraded forest in Gishwati is
similarly an important catchment area for the Sebaya system which feeds the
hydroelectric plant in Gisenyi. The rivers flowing out of Nyungwe and
Gishwati provide drinking and irrigation water for farmers residing below
the forested area. The forests play a similarly important role in
mitigating soil erosion acknowledged as one of the primary factors in
decreasing soil fertility in Rwanda's hilly landscape. Their elimination
would, therefore, lead to disruption of streamflow, substantial increase in
soil erosion and a massive increase in sedimentation.
6.03     The forests also serve to maintain biological diversity.  Nyungwe
as the richest block of natural forest left in Rwanda is an extremely
valuable conservation area. Although its faunal components have been little
studied, its diverse plant life constitutes a reserve of gene pool material
that has future potential for maintaining agricultural production. The
forest harbors a great variety of animal species, including some endangered
primate species.  Given the probability that many large mammals currently
existing in smaller forest relics will become extinct, the large size of the
forest distinguishes it as a refuge where such animals may be able to
survive in the long-term. The Gishwati forest also contains a significant
diversity and density of lower Afro-montane primary forest species. It
harbors various wildlife species. Some of this wildlife, as is the case in
Nyungwe, are endangered species. The project's investments in forest
protection and conservation coupled with enactment of stronger forest
protection laws would help preserve this wildlife.
6.04     Other benefits difficult to quantify would stem from the proAject's
institutional development activities. The project would serve as an
important instrument for implementing the Government's forestry sector
policy and would further refine the scope of that policy. The project. would
strengthen DGF's planning and supervisory powers through the implementation
of the monitoring and evaluation programs and the training of needed
technical and managerial personnel. Through its wood pricing and marketing
study, the land use study and complementary livestock sector study, the
project would result in the definition and implementation of more
appropriate policies on the pricing and marketing of wood and dairy
products. The project would help initiate GOR's policy of integrating; the
project approach to development more firmly into sectoral programs. It
would further increase private sector involvement in the implementation of
investment activities which would have a significant impact on the
Government's recurrent cost budget.
6.05     Direct project beneficiaries would total over 75,000 farm families.
An estimated 30,000 families would benefit from project extension and animal
health care act vities. About 1,300 families would benefit from pasture
activities. An additional 45,000 families are expected to benefit from
seedling production and distribution and promotion of agro-forestry through
training. Many project activities in plantation establishment,



- 42 -
infrastructure development and pasture and plantation maintenance are labor
intensive. These activities would generate about 9,000 man-years of rural
employment over the project investment period.
B. Economic Analysis
6.06     An overall ezonomic rate of re&urn (ERR) has not been estimated for
the project because: (a) the majority of project investment (67%) 's in
activities whose benefits are difficult to quantify such as natural forest
protection, and institutional development or where the speed of accrual of
benefits is uncertain, e.g., extension; (b) although focussing on forestry
development, project activities are varied in nature and difficult to lump
together ranging as they do from plantation and pasture maintenance to farm
level extension activities aimed at integrating forestry, livestock and
agriculture. Rates of return have, however, been calculated for those
activities whose benefits are more readily quantifiable. The ERRs are
presented in Table 6.1 and range from 162 for Gishwati new plantations to
45% for Gishwati phase I pasture maintenance. Details of the analysis are
given in Annex 9.
Table 6.1: Results of Economic Analysis
Component                                     % of Base Cost       ERR (%)
Kigali-Butare fuelwood Plantations Maintenance    8                        40
Gishwati Phase I plantation Ma3ntenance              1                     28
Gishwati new plantation establishment                6                     16
Gishwati Phase I Pasture Maintenance                18                     45
6.07     The cost-benefit analysis (ignoring the without project scenarios)
for the maintenance components show a 36Z ERR for the Kigali-Butare
fuelwood plantation maintenance component, 25Z for Gishwati plantation
maintenance and a 12% ERR for the Gishwati pasture component. The
differenre between the incremental and cost benefit analysis for the latter
component is based oz. the assumption (for which there is field evidence)
that the pastures would be overstocked with low productivity Ankole cattle
and would deteriorate rapidly.
6.08     The cost streams used in estimating the ERRs include all project
investment and incremental operating costs and recurrent costs needed post-
project to sustain project activities. Project benefits include all of the
incremental project output above the estimated "without project" situation for
each activity. Economic prices of project output are shown in Table 5.1.
Prices of inputs are given in Annex 9. Prices of non-traded goods were
adjusted by the standard conversion factor (0.85) or by specific conversion
fac-ors. The opportunity cost of labor was estimated at RF74 (74% of the
stipulated minimum wage).



- 43 -
6.09     For the extension component (142 of base costs) where the speed of
accrual of benefits is uncertain, a judgment on the soundness of the
investment was made by carrying out an indicative calculation of the order of
magnitude of benefits nLeeded to produce a 15% ERR and the likelihood of
attaining those benefits.  If the net incremental production from extension
activities were to reach $1.0 million per year by subproject year 12
discounted economic benefits would equal discounted costs and the ERR would be
15% (Annex 9).  An average 5% per annum production increase (over 7 years) and
an average 3% yearly adoption rate would ensure attainm.nt of the Lequired
level of benefits.  These production increases and adoption rates w,uld be
readily attainable.

C. Risks
6.11     The major risk associated with the project is institutional and is
related primarily to the Gishwat4 pasture component. The project will
attempt to correct implementation and management errors arising from Phase
I. Part of this will involve searching for new approaches to pasture



- 44 -
development and to prevention of encroachment of cattle in the forest
(para. 3.19). It will also involve putting in place appropriate policies
for dairy processing, marketing and pricing. The project will assist the
Government to implement an ambitious pasture cost recovery program and to
privatize the management of investments in Gishwati. As part of the
process of carrying out the above institutional reforms, the project will
also assist GOR to phase out the well-established GBK project unit. These
are difficult reforms that require the Government's commitment and will to
succeed in the face of probable social and political pressure. GOR has
expressed a commitment to the institutional reforms. Assurances were
obtained on reorienting management of the Gishwati component (para. 4.08,
4.11), on implementing the new levels of cost recovery (para. 5.15) and on
gradual transfer of the management of GBK's activities to other agencies
and private entities (para. 4.11).
6.12     Another important area of risk relates to the capacity of DGE to
provide adequate t lchnical supervision for the protection and conservation
activities in Nyungwe and to assist the Conservation Center (when this is
set up) to coordinate donor activities. DGF is a relatively new department
with a limited number of staff of managerial calibre (para. 2.10). To
minimize the risk of inadequate techkdical supervision, the project would
provide technical assistance during the project build-up period to assist
DGF in designing a sound ecological inventory and natural resources
monitoring and evaluation program. During project implementation, a full
time ecologist would be retained to implement and manage the protection and
conservation activities and to train Rwandese foresters to carry on after
the project period (para. 4.04). With regard to coordination of external
aid agencies in Nyungwe, assurances were obtained on donor coordination as
part of the terms of reference of the proposed conservation center. (para.
4.04).
6.13     The project will require substantial supervision by GOR and IDA to
monitor the effectiveness of the reforms being proposed. Strengthening of
monitoring and evaluation has been built into project design to generate
the information necessary for effective planning and supervision.
Supervision should focus on the managerial and technical aspects of the
Gishwati pasture component, the implementation of the extension component,
and the technical soundness of the natural forest protection and management
component. Supervision of the financial aspects of the project will also
be important.
D. Environmental Impact
6.14     The proposed project would have a highly beneficial environmental
impact. Virtually all components are designed with environmental concerns
as an important objective. The natural forest protection and conservation
component would assure conservation of Rwanda's remaining Afro-montane
forests which harbor important wildlife including some endangered species.
It would also help decrease soil erosion and sedimentation and assure
continued availability of drinking and irrigation water for farmers
residing below the forested areas. The proposed land use study in the
Gishwati component would focus on decreasing encroachment and degradation
in the natural forest. Establishment of plantations on denuded hilltops
would help mitigate soil erosion while the development of extension themes
on the ititegration of forestry into the agriculture and livestock farming
systems would also assist in efforts to prevent soil erosion and maintain
soil fertility. A small amount of insecticideswould be used for plant



- 45 -
protection in the extension component. These would be restricted to those
which have proven effective in the local context and have no known negative
environmental effects.
E. Impact on Women
6.15     The project would have a positive impact on women through its farm
forestry and extension activities. Women and children currently bear the
burden of searching for fuelwood and fodder when on-farm supplies are
inadequate. By promoting the integration of multipurpose agroforestry
species in the farming systems and providing appropriate seedlings the
project would help to increase the availability of fuelwood and fodder
close to home and would lessen the amount of energy spent by women on these
tasks.



- 46 -
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED
7.01     Prior to negotiations agreements were reached on the following:
(a) For Gishwati, mandatory destocking by June 30, 1987 of Ankole
cattle in partial exchange for pure breed cattle in the ratio
of three to one (para. 3.16);
(b) The GBK project unit would as of February 1, 1987 no longer
extend credit to livestock owners or CAT at subsidized rates
(para 3,16);
(c) Maintenance of a permanent herd of 150 pure breed cows at CAT
by December 31, 1987 (para. 3.16);
(d) Proper sanitary control (quarantine) of animals destined for
the Gishwati pastures by December 31, 1987 (para. 3.16);
(e) Autonomy for the Gishwati sub-project director in the
technical management of subproject activities and
decentralization to Gishwati of the management of CAT, "zone
d'embouche", veterinary phatmacy and dairy by September 30,
1987 (para. 3.17);
(f) Housing provided to higher level staff would be subject to a
rental fee sufficient to enable the project to cover the
maintenance cost of buildings. Motorcycles provided to
extension personnel would be paid for through monthly salary
deductions to be determined by MINAGRI with the funds
collected going for the purchase of new vehicles (para 3.24);
(g) GOR - IDA review end 1989 of the results of pilot pasture
rehabilitation in Mutara (para. 3.27);
(h) Not later than January 31, 1988 the GBK project unit and DGF
would sign contracts with ISAR on implementation of applied
research activities. These contracts should clearly spell out
the responsibilities of each party (paras. 4.05 and 4.11);
(i) Periodic review and adjustment of the milk producer price in
line with demand pending completion of the livestock sector
study (para. 5.05) and enlargement of the distribution and
marketing channels for Gishwati milk;
(j) An increase in the level of cost recovery on pastures and
revision by credit effectiveness of old rental contracts in
line with the new cost recovery schedule ,para 5.15);

a management contract would be drawn up between the communes
and Minagri clearly stating communes' responsibilities and
assuring that plantation land be kept as forest land and
harvesting guidelines observed (paea. 4.10).
(r) The GBK project unit would be completely phased out by the end
of 1992 in accordance with an action plan acceptable to IDA
(para 4.11).
(s) Arrangements on annual work plans, annual review, quarterly
and annual progress reports and the project completion report
would be followed (paras. 4.13, 4.14, 4.15 and 4.16).
(t) For purposes of cost recovery, agreements satisfactory to IDA
will be entered into between GOR and the revenue-earning
entities and GOR and pasture beneficiaries (paras. 5.11 and
5.15).
7.03     The following is a condition of board presentation:
(a) Start-up of the wood supply, demand, pricing and marketing
study and the study and design of the Nyungwe ecological



- 49 -
inventory and natural resources monitoring and evaluation
program (paras 2.04 and 3.09).
7.04     The following are conditions of credit effectiveness:
(a) Constitution of a local commission to demarcate legal
boundaries for the Nyungwe buffer plantations and demarcation
of the boundaries (para. 3.07);
(b) Establishment by GOR of the counterpart fund bank account and
three subaccounts in the National Bank of Rwanda in the name
of GBK, Nyungwe and DGF implementing units and deposit of
intial amounts corresponding to approximately four months
expenditures for each component (para. 3.37);
(c) Recruitment of the accounting and financial management expert
for the Nyungwe project unit, establishment of the DGF
accounting system and recruitment of an AO accountant (para.
3.38);
(d) Establishment of the Nyungwe project unit. Recruitment of an
AO project manager, an ecologist and an AO forester for the
unit (para. 4.04);
(e) Reduction to a level satisfactory to the Association in the
personnel of the GBK project unit and the revenue earning
entities according to an action plan agreed with IDA (paras.
4.08, 4.10);
(f) Development by GOR of a pricing mechanism for milk acceptable
to IDA (para 5.05);
(g) GOR would, as a first step, prepare a modified rental contract
for the Gishwati pastures acceptable to IDA, and as a second
step, present evidence satisfactory to IDA that pasture
beneficiaries on already established pastures have signed the
contract (para 5.15).
7.05     The following are conditions of disbursements:
(a) Joint GOR-IDA agreement on a course of actiox, for dairy
processing based on the recomnendations of the livestock SPPF study. No
funds would be disbursed for development of the Gishwati dairy beyond 5,000
liters per day processing capacity without such an agreement (para. 3.16).
(b) Disbursement of US$800,000 for new approaches to
land/livestock development in Gishwati would be conditional on
joint GOR-IDA review and agreement on a course of action for
land development based on the recommendations of the land use
study (para 3.19).
7.06     With the above assurances and conditions, the proposed project
would be suitable for an IDA credit of US$14.1 million to the Government of
Rwanda.

ANNEX 3
RWANDA
SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
PROTECTION OF THE NYUNGWE AND GISHWATI NATURAL FORESTS
This annex and the following ones of the other Gishwati components
and on the farm farestry component provide a degree of detail which is
interm.ediate between the working papers and the Detailed Features section
of the Project chapter of the main test.
The Government of Rwanda has asked IDA and other development
agen_ies to help it protect the Nyungwe forest, and it has asked IDA to
help it protect also the Gishwati forest. The reason why the Government
wishes to protect these forests rather than allow farmers to clear them for
agriculture, as they cleared most of the forest which once covered Rwanda,
is that it considers the potential agricultural value of the remaining
natural forests to be less than their scientific, cultural, soil and water
conservation, and gene pool values. The forests also have a potential
value for tourism, including scientific tourism. Their potential timber
production value, however, is slight.
T. PROTECTION OF THE NYUNGWE FOREST
At present 100% of the Nyungwe forest is a Reserve, although the
resources of the Government does not allow effective protection. In an
"Action Plan for the Conservation and Management of the Natural Forests on
the Zaire-Nile Divide" published by the Forestry Department in September
1984 it was proposed that the forest be divided for protection purposes
into four zones or units, and that the support of a different agency be
sought for each unit. The following agencies have now agreed to support
the protection of the various units: the Swiss PPF project would support
the protection of Unit ("Unite de Gestion Zone", UGZ) 1, In the north; the
European Development Fund (FED) - Unit 2, in the northeast; the Caisse
Centrale de Cooperation Economique - UlnIt 4, in the southwest. It is now
proposed that IDA, through the Forestry II project, should finance the
protection of Unit 3, situated in the southeast of the forest. See Map No.
A Swiss project, "Project Pilote Forestier", has been active in
Rwandf for more than a decade. It has agreed to help protect the northern
sector of the Nyungwe forest, Unit 1. Its main activities in 1986/1987
will be to put in place the administrative -.ructures for Unit 1, to begin
the construction of the Unit 1 center at gisovu, and to initiate their
management-oriented inventory ("inventaire pour l'amenagement"). The FED
project, "Projet Crete Zaire-Nil (CNZ)", has been active in its zone for
the last 4 years, and has already established 3,000 ha of buffer
plantation, mainly of Acacia melanoxylon and Pinus patula. The Caisse
Centrale de Cooperation Economique has agreed to lend Rwanda 40 million
French francs to finance the protection of Unit 4.  Disbursement is
beginning now, Rnd will last three years. Part of this loan will be used
to construct the conservation center.
The proposed conservation center would be situated approximattly
in the middle of the forest, along the Butare-Cyangugu road where the four



ANNEX 3
Units come together. The main objective of this center would be
protection, and scientific research would be an important secondary
objective. There would be houses for forest guards, for a forest
conservator, a police officer, and visiting scientists. There would also
be offices and a meeting room. The Caisse Centrale loan would finance the
investment costs, including training, while the Forest Department would
finance the operating costs.
The Action Plan furthermore proposed that 40% of the forest should
remain as a Reserve, while 60% should be opened up to management. In a
position paper distributed during the post-appraisal mission in September-
October 1986 to the Government and to the other agencies involved in the
protection of the Nyungwe forest, the IDA mission made clear its strong
conviction that the management should be of the lightest possible kind, anid
that in particular the contruction of internal roads should be limited to a
minimum. The mission has been assured by the Government and by the other
donors that commercial exploitation is not envisaged in any part of the
forest. (In any case, such exploitation would probably not ev?n repay the
cost of roads and logging, let alone leave a net surplus for stumpage.)
The only exploitation that will be allowed would be some controlled
customary harvesting of fuelwood and building poles from the forest fringe
areas, by the population immediately atround the forest.
A. Establishment of Forest Station (Implementation Unit)
The unit to be protected by the project has an area of about
25,000 ha, i.e. about a quarter of the total forest. The project would
establish a station near the southeastern sector of the Nyungwe forest
(Unit 3), for the implementation of its various activities in connection
with the protection of the forest: :he 900 ha buffer strip plantation, the
patrolling of the plantation and the forest by guards, the ecological
inventory, the inititation of the research and monitoring program, and the
extension work among the populatior, adjacent to the forest. The estimated
cost of the two main Nyungwe components - the activities related to the
buffer plantation, and the activitles related to the ecological inventory -
are set out in Tables            of :he Appraisal Report.
The forest station would be situated for example at Tangabo in
Kivu Commune, or at Busenyi, Wingugu Sector, Mudasomwa Commune. Busenyi,
about 2 km from the forest and about the same distance to the south of the
Kitabi tea factory, would probably be cheaper to supply with electricity
and water. It would also have better communications to the University of
Butare, which could provide useful support to the ecological inventory,
especially with regard to species identification. Busenyi would also have
better communications to the proposed Conservation Center to be financed by
the Caisse Centrale and to be situated inside the forest, along the Butare-
Cyangugu main road.
Besides the main station to be established near the northern part
of the Unit, smaller outstations would be established in the Commune of
Kivu, near the central part of the Unit, and in the Comm.une of Nshili, near
the sourhorn part.



- 66 -                           ANNEX 3
B. Buffer Plantation
A strip of plantation 200 m wide and 45 km long, i.e. with a total
area of some 900 ha, will be established along the border of the natural
forest to demarcate the latter and to serve as a psychological barrier to
encroachment.  The soils in the area are generally poor, and Pinus patula
is one of the few species that can be grown successfully on most of the
sites. On the better sites Cupressus lusitanica and Acacia melanoxylon
should be grown.
The indigenous species Polyscias fulva ("Umwungo") should also be
planted.  It is a very fast-growing local pioneer species with an excellent
form, and producing a good matchwood for which there is a ready market at
the match factory in Butare, 70 km away.  ISAR has recently sold Polyscias
logs with a diameter of 30-40 cm on roadside in its arboretum for FRW
12,000/m3 to the match factory, but for standing timber at Nyungwe the
price will be much less. Although Polyscias yields an excellent matchwood,
there would probably not be a market for its thinnings.  To nllow it to
grow as rapidly as possible to diameters where it can be sold as matchwood,
it should therefore be planted far apart (about 3.5 to 4.0 meters), and the
weed growth should be kept down by allowing taungya intercropping. For the
farmers to be interested in intercropping, the soil would have to be
reasonably good. And for the match factory to be interested, the trees
should be grown in reasonably accessible areas in the northern part of the
buffer strip. Polyscias should therefore be planted on relatively good and
accessible sites.
With a target stocking at planting of 1100 seedlings per hectare
for most species, a 20% allowance for culling and another 20% for replacing
dead trees, and 900 ha of buffer plantation plus the equivalent of 25 ha of
roadside plantation, altogether about 1.5 million seedlings would be
needed. When these seedlings can be produced more cheaply by private
contractors than by the project itself, this activity will be privatized.
Besides entrepreneurs, sector nurseries a,-d group nurseries, schools should
also be approached in this connection. The vocational (CERAI) schools in
particular could produce seedlings, as practical agricultural work is an
Important part of their curriculum. Each Commune has at least one CERAI.
One possible problem is that the CERAIs are closed for the vacation from
July until the middle of September.
As the climate of Nyungwe is favorable for tree-growing, exotic
seedlings should be raised as bare-rooted stock using the Swaziland system,
which is used in the FED project next door, and which reduces the cost per
seedling from FRW 3-4 to FRW 1. Only for the more delicate indigenous
seedlings would the traditional plastic bags or pots be used.
The following plantinig schedule is envisaged:
1988   1989   1990   1991   1992   Torta
Bufret plantation, ha                          150    300    300    150    900
Roadside plantings, ha-equivalent   _ 8    _ 6           6      5    _         25
Totals:                    8    156    306    305    150    925



- 67 -                             ANNEX 3
The total direct cost of establishing these 925 ha would be FRW
33,450,000 (see cost table), i.e. FRW 36,162 (USS 425) per ha. This would
comprise seedling production, nursery material, site preparation, planting,
replacing dead plants, weeding and road construction. Although road
construction would be kept to a minimum of 60 km (along the edge of the
forest, not inside the forest), it would amount to as much as 45% of the
total direct cost. Labor intensive methods would be used, requiring
altogether 310,600 man-days, and only about 8% of the total direct cost
would be represented by non-labor items such as nursery materials and hand
tools.
The following wood production from the buffer plantation has been
assumed in the economic analysis:
1997-  2003-  2008-  2013-  2018-  2023-  2028-
2002   2007   2012   2017   2022   2027   2031
Cubic meters    2250   4500   9000  15750  27000  45000  58500
This volume production corresponds to a total of 162,000 m3 over the 35
years harvesting period or che 45 years total growing period.  This in turn
corresponds to 8 m3/ha/year on the 50Z of the 900 ha which are considered
productive. The reason why the other 50% of the area would not be
considered productivo., would generally be steep slopes or inaccessibility.
On such sites the function of the buffer plantation would thus be purely
protective.
C. Ecolopical  Inventory
In October 1986 the Forestry Department published a provisional
direct've for the proposed inventory of the natural forests of the Zaire-
Nile divide. It states that topographical maps with contour lines will be
available in a year's time, with a scale of 1:10,000 or even 1:5,000.
Aerial photographs from 1980 and 1982 are also available. Five
stratifications are proposed for the vegetation maps. It is proposed that
the inventories be carried out in two stages; a pre-inventory for planning
purposes, and the main inventory. Sampling percentages are suggested.
Variables of forest, soil and fauna to be inventoried are outlined.
Personnel and equipment required for the inventory are described. Although
these inventories would thus take note also of soil and fauna, they would
mainly be conventional timber inventories.
As the purpose of the Nyungwe component of the proposed IDA
project, is to protect the forest, one would wish to have a more complece
knowledge of what It Is that *he project would protect. Such know.edge
shouid comprise far more than could be provided by a conventional timber
Inventory. The latter gives information by species on heights, diameters,
volumes and stem form. Such information is useful for loggers, but not for
forest protection. Instead, the project therefore proposes to finance an
ecological inventory of the plant and animal species of the forest, the
factors which threaten it (mining, poaching, agr.cultural encroachment,
road-buildirig, fires, etc.), and of the forest dynamics, i.a. of the



A8Nf:X 3
principles which govern changes in the forest, either away from the
original climax vegetation, or back towards that ultimate goal. Another
important objective of the ecological inventory would be to quantify as far
as possible the benefits yielded by the natural forests, to provide a
rationale for their protection.
To undertake this ecological inventory, the project would finance
an ecologist for five years. The post-appraisal mission was Liformed by
the FAC represtentative in Kigali that FAC will probably be able to finance
this post, i.e. to cofinance the ptoject. Besides the ecological
inventory, the ecologist would set up an ecological monitoring and
evaluation system to follow the later development of the forest, and draw
up a research program for the Nyingwe forest in close cooperation with ISAR
in Butare. When the project would come to an end in 1992, the monitoring
and evaluation would become the direct responsibilty of the Conservation
Center, which would report on this matter to the Forestry Department's
Bureau of Forest Inventory and Statistics. The research program would be
arried out by ISAR. The ecologist would undertake a similar work program
in the smaller Gishwati natural forest (Section II below).
There have already been a number of studies of the Nyurngwe forest.
In order not to waste resources by re-doing what has already been done, the
ecologist's first task should be to undertake a thorough literature study.
Part of this can be done already outside Rwanda. The rest can be done with
the guidance of ISAR, the University of Butare, and the Swiss forestry
project in Rwanda, as most of the studies have bhen carried out by those
three organizations.
The proposed ecological inventory would cover not only the areas
earmarked for maniagement in the Forestry Department's previously mentione(d
1984 Action Plan for the natural forests, but also the areas earmarked as
Reserves. Furthermore, the project would be prepared to finance such an
inventory not only in its own Ut,i- 3, but also - if the Forestry Departmeent
so wishes - to fill possible ecological information gaps not covered by the
inventories which will be carried out in the other Units.
As the threats to the forest are closely linked to sociological
factors, the ecologist would be supported during his first year by a socio-
economic adviser, who should have a good knowledge of local rural sociology
and of the local language, Kinyarwanda. Hopefully, a local expert could be
found for the latter post, e.g. at the University of Butare. In the
costing for the ecological inventory, 16 consultant-months are also
Included. This would allow further inputs by the socio-economist during
years 2-5.
The detailed work plan for the ecologist and the socio-economist
will be drawn up by a 2/3-month mission by an ecologist and a socio-
economist in early 1987, to be paid from Pre-Project Finance (PPF).
Suggested profiles of the ecologist and the socio-economist have been sent
to the Government. The same profiles can be used for recruiting the above-
mentiorned PPF mission.
Inventory is not research, and although proximity to ISAR and t.0
the University of Butare would have been useful for the ecologist, the



- 69 -                            ANNEX 3
post-appraisal mission came to the conclusion, after discussions with
relevant ISAR and Forestry Department officials, that proximity to the
forest and its scheduled Conservation Center would be even more useful. It
is therefore proposed that the ecologist be based at the station which the
project would established near Unit 3.
One of the first requirements for the ecological inventory would
be to acquire good recent aerial or satellite photographs or imagery. FRW
16.9 million has been included in the project for this purpose, but it can
hopefully be done for considerably less, as the Forestry Department
document "Directives (Provisoires) pour l'Inventaire des Forets Naturelles
de la Crete Zaire-Nil", October 1986, states that topographical maps with
contour lines, at a scale of 1:10,000 and even 1:5,000 will be available
within one year, and that aerial photograophs taken in 1980 and 1982 can be
put at the disposal of the Units.
D. Other Actitivities to be Carried out trom the Unit 3 Station
The project would finance ten forest guards to patrol the buffer
plantation and the natural forest. As the project would not finance any
roads inside the forest, the guards would have to patrol the latter on foot
or, if and where the quality of footpaths allow it, on bicycle. (In
tropical areas with high population pressure, such as Rwanda, forest roads
eventually and inevitably become conduits of destrution, even if they were
originally constructed for protection.)
The technical staff at the station - one AO forester, two A2, and
two A3 - would also be responsible for liaising with the local extension
services staff to sensitize the population living near the forest. This
population has a customary right to take fuelwood and poles from the forest
for their own use. This can continue, but under the control of the
forestry staff, who should advice the local population on which species to
take and which to leave alone, on the necessity to avoid wasteful
exploitation, and on the value of the forest to them in particular and to
the country in general.
Due to the scientific interest of Nyungwe forest, it can be
expected that scientists will wish to visit it, and the proposed forest
station would be able to provide some limited accomodation to such
visitors, whose work could be valuable for the project.
IT. GISHWATI FOREST
The 1980 Staff Appraisal Report for the first phase of the
Integrated Forestry and Livestock Development Project reported that only
about 5,000 ha of relatively untouched natural forest remained of the total
25,000-28,000 ha of the Gishwati "Forest" Reserve, and six years later the
forestry consultant participating in the appraisal mission for the second
phase stated that this area "is thotught to be still largely intact". In
fact the line of Eucalyptus maidenii trces which was planted already in



- 70 -
ANNEX 3
Colonial times to indicate the boundary of the natural forest seems to have
been generally respected. The forest is nevertheless under considerable
pressure, both from within and from without. Within, it is being degraded
by some twenty thousand cattle which graze there, ard for which there seems
to be nowhere else to go. From without it is threatened by large-scale
clearing of the degraded bamboo and scrub forest whiclh has taken place
during the last two years or less, by forest workers and by independent
farmers, mainly for the cultivation of potatoes.
The first phase pastures and forest plantations were established
at some distance to the northeast of the natural forest, but the 2,000 ha
of additional industrial ,lantation which has been proposed for the second
phase would probably be established along part of the natural forest
boundary, and would provide protection for that part.   For the protection
of the remainder o' the boundary, with an estimated length of about 9 km,
the project would .'inance the establishement of a 200-ha buffer plantation.
The project would also finance 6 additional forest guards to
patrol the natural forest, besides the 19 additional guards for the
proposed new 2,000 ha of industrial plantation. As it is expected that
part of the latter would be adjacent to the natural forest, the guards
patrollirng the plantations would also strengthen the protection of the
natural forest.
The soils at Gishwati are considerably better than at Nyungwe, and
more demanding species can be used. It is proposed that the 200 ha of
buffer plantation be comprised of 70 ha of Cupressus lusitanica, another 70
ha of Acacia melanoxylon, and 60 of indigenous species (see Appendix II of
working paper for a list of potential indigenous species). It is further
proposed that the buffer plantation be established over the five years
1988-92 at an average rate of 40 ha/year. Funds have been included to
allow the production of traditional potted seedlings, but considering the
excellent planting conditions at Gishwati it would be sufficient to use
bare-rooted seedlings which can be produced by means of the Swaziland
sustem at about a third of the cost. The project staff can learn this
method from the FED-funded project which is supporting the protection of
the northeast Unit of the Nyungwe forest.
As indicated in the cost table for the Gishwati buffer plantation,
the total direct cost of establishing this plantations is estimated at FRW
9,569,200, i.e. FRW 47,846 (US$563) per ha. This would comprise seedling
production, nursery materials, site preparation, planting, replacement of
dead plants, weeding and road construction. The last item would account
for 26% of the total. Of the total direct cost, 95% would be for inskilled
labor.
The main purpose of the buffer plantation would be to prot,act the
natural forest, and any possible production would be purely incidental.  It
has been assumed that 50% of the plantation would not be harvested, mainly
due to inaccessibility, and that the other half would yield 17,000 m3 of
sawlogs and 5,500 m3 of poles over a period of 30 years. Due to the
relatively small Irvestment involved, the rate of return of the Gishwati
buffer plantation was not calculated.

- 78 -                             Annex 4
RWANDA
SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
AGROFORESTRY AND RURUAL AFFORESTATION
The proposed project would support tree planting by farmers for
fuelwood ard other purposes rather than the plantation approach to fuelwood
development pursued in Phase I. The tree planting by the farmers could be
on a communal or an individual basis, and in either case it could be in the
form of single trees, lines of trees or bushes (hedges, windbreaks, road
trees, boundary lines, etc.), or conventional plantations. Some of these
forms of tree planting, even the plantations, could be in close association
with crops (agroforestry), or with crops and grazing(agrosilvo-
pastoralism', or with grazing only (silvopastoralism). Such combinations
are traditiornal in Rwanda. The possible permutations of these various
forms of tree planting are virtually innumerable. They will here be
designiated under the umbrella term "farm forestry".
The project would support farm forectry in sixty Sectors
("Sec.eurs") in the Kigali and Butare prefectures, where the project would
in any case be active in the maintenance of the Phase I plantations, and
where other projects ("DGB" in Butare Prefecture, "Kigali Nord", "Kigali
Est", "Project Shyorongi" etc. in Kigali Prefecture) are not already
supporting farm forestry.  The 60 Sectors would be situated in the Communes
of GIshamvu and Maraba in Butare Prefecture, and in Bicumbi, Gikomero,
Gikoro, Mbogo, Mugambazi and Rutongo in K'gali Prefecture.
The support to farm forestry in the 60 Sectors would take two main
forms: support to seedling production and support to tree planting. The
support t-? seedling production would again take two main forms: support to
the traditional Sector nurseries, and support to group nurseries
('pepinieres ce groupement") and other private nurseries.  Each of these
will be dealt with in turn.



-79-                               Annex 4
A. Sector Nurseries
In the Sector nurseries the Forestry Department traditionally
provides seed, plastic bags, watering cans and other implements, and one
nursery worker per nurse;y, while the local community provides the
remaining labor, and the land on which the nursery is situated. Sector
nurseries in the Kigali and Butare Prefectures generally produce 30-40
incentives which get the local communities involved in seedling production.
These inputs, and especiilly the presence of the nursery workers also allow
the Forestry Department to introduce new species which tne rural population
would not become acquainted with if left to its own devices. The most
popular species with farmers in the Kigali and Butare Prefectures is
eucalyptus, followed by Grevillea robusta. The farmers should also be
introduced to nitrogen-fixing, soil-improving species like Leucaena
leucocephala, an excellent fodder and fuelwood species, and Acacia mearnsii
(Black Wattle) much maligned because of its profuse growth, but excellent
for fuelwood and charcoal.
B.  grouNurseries and Other Private Nurseries
In certain Sectors a Proup of farmers may wish to estaLlish their
own nursery. Such nurseries would generally be smaller than the sector
nurseries, each producing about 5,000 seedlings a year, and would therefore
not justify the presence of a nursery worker. The other inputs given to
the sector nurseries, however, would also be given to the group nurseries.
For the purposes of costing, ;t will be assumed that in the 60 Sectors, 20
such groups would express an interest in establishing their own nurseries
during each of the five project implementation years, so that at the end of
the period 100 such nurseries would have been established, producing about
500,000 seedlings a yeir.  This may be an op.imistic estimate, and the
project funds that will be allocated for this purpose should therefore
suffice to support also other private ir.itiatives that may be encouraged in
the field of seedling production.
Where there is a market for seedlings, for example where the
Forestry Department can guarantee such a market, schools may be interested
in producing seedlings. This will be explored for the project's
reforestation components, but none of these is situated in the Kigali and
Butare Prefectures.

that this species is not suited to the very poor soils in this region.
Instead, less demanding eucalypts like E. camaldulensis, E. paniculata and
E. maidenii shouild be produced, besides the previously mentioned
agroforestry species. Guidance on suitable plantation species is best
obtained from existing good stands on similar sites in the region.

-84 -                              Annex 4
RUiRAL AFFORESTATION MONITORING
AND EVALUATION PROGRAM
BACKGROUND
One of the objectives of the second project phase would be to
continue the institution-building of the Ministry of Agriculture begun
under the first phase.  In line with this objective the project would
strengthen the existing management information system.
PURPOSE OF A MONITORING AND EVALUATION PROGRAM
The purpose of an MIS is to provide feedback of information to
management, to help the latter in its decision-making.  Existing accounts,
annual reports etc. are all part of the MIS.  Today, however, the advent of
computers has greatly increased our ability to store and retrieve data, and
thereby to provide management with up-to-date and relevant information.
IMPLEMENTATION
It is recommended that a Rwandese professional forester (AO) with
a good knowledge of English be sent to Kenya and Malawi for two months to
study the MIS which have been introduced at the forestry departments there
with the support of the World Bank and FAO. He should then be given
siother month to write a report on his findings, with recommendations on
how a similar system could be introduced into the Rwanda Forestry
Department. The total duration of his assignment would thus be three
months.
To ensure that his knowledge is not lost, a post should be created
for him in the Forestry Department's Bureau of Forest Inventory and
Statistics, where he would be in charge of the MIS. He should prepare a
list of data proposed for inclusion in the MIS. As the objective of the
MIS is to provide relevant fevd back information for the decision-makers,
the latter should vet the liL._ to ensure that valuable resources are not
used for collecting irrelevant information.
When the UNDP/FAO project for the strengthening of the Ministry of
Agriculture is in place, that project should be asked to help develop the
MIS further.
INFORMATION ON FARM FORESTRY
Due to the large number of projects involved in farm forestry
("Arborisation Rurale"; Including agroforestry, village woodlots etc.), and
the different approaches followed by these projects, this would be a
particularly important field for the MIS to collect information on. Such
information should cover for example: different approaches to cost
recovery, proportion of farmers who undertake treeplanting in different
sectors, preferred species, survival rates, annual volume growth of free-
growing trees and of stands, and prices of fuelwood, charcoal and poles.



- 85 -
Annex 4
REQUIREMENTS
Although the detailed list of requirements will have to await the
forester's study tour to Kenya and Malawi, and the recommendations of the
UNDP/FAO project, it is clear that at least the following resources are
required to set up and operate an MIS within the Forestry Department:
One AO Forester to head the unit.
12 field enumerators to collect the basic information.
One or two Al or A2 foresters to supervise the field enumerators.
A computer for data storage and retrieval.
A secretary/typist to feed the data into the computer, and to do other
secretarial work.
Typewriter, stationery, office equipment anl furniture.
A vehicle for the foresters and bicycles for the field enumerators.

Etude de Commercialisation et de marche du bois
1.       Pendant une mission de 2 mois debutant le ler janvier 1987,
l'economiste forestier et le specialiste en comm,iercialization
entreprendront une etude de l'offre et de la demande du bois, la fixation
de prix et le commercialisation du bois au Rwanda. L'objectif de cette
etude sera de rassembler une base solide de donnees necessaires pour des
decisions en ce qui concerne la politique du commercialisation, prix et
marche du bois.  L'etude se concentrera sur le bois de feu charbon de bois,
les perches, le bois scie et le bois industriel et, pour ce dernier,
specialement le pin.

les ministres techniques concernes, les projets forestiers finances par

devront rester en liaison avec la Mission Residente de la Banque Mondiale.
La DGF leur fournira tout le support logistique necessaire (transport et
travaux de secretariat). A la fin de leur mission, ils remettront un

des exportatr.ons de bois de pin sci  au Kivu, Zaire.

GISHWATI PHASE II PASTURE DEVELOPMENT
1.       The first phase project developed an estimated 3400 ha of
pastures. These pastures are not all at the same stage of completion: 680
ha has been cleared a:d remains to be pla.ited with Kikuyu grass in April,
May. June of 1987.  This activity will be financed with government
c~ounterpart funding. An additional 920 ha of pastures has already recently
been planted with Kikuyu grass but requires high maintenance during the
next two years. This cost will be born by the GOR in 1987 and shared by
IDA (70%) and the GOR (30%) in 1988. Galculations made on a 30 ha farm
model indicate that a pasture can generate sufficient reveniues to Lover its
operating costs i7hen its milk production reaches 30 liters per day. On
average this prcduction can be reached within the two years following the
first stocking with cattle. For this reason the project will only continue
to finance pascure maintenance costs until a pasture reaches a daily milk
production of 30 liters or two years after the date of first cattle
stocking whichever comes first. The veterinary personnel will consist of
mne A2 level veterindrian per 15 pastures (450 ha) and 2 assistant
veterfnarian per 5 pastures (150 ha'. The A2 Veterinarian personnel will
be financed by the project for a period of 3 years following the date of
first cattle stocking in order to ensure that qualified personnel will be
kept during this period. rhe two assistant veterinarian per 5 pastures
will be project financed until pastures reach a daily mi±k production of 30
liters a day or two years following the date of first cattle stocking
whichever comes first. Given the complicated nature of managing a herd cc
pure breed cattle and .he inexperience in Rwanda of such management,
technical assistance will be provided and IDA financed for the recruitment
of a very experienced zootechnician/veterinarian who will be the director
of the Gishwati compc-nent. Technical assistance will also be required 'o
assist in the establishment of a federation of cattle owners by the end of
1989. This assistance would consist of a locally recruited expert in 1987
and 1988 and of an internationally recruited expert in 1989 whose role will
be to recommend an organiz- ional structure and set up for the federation
and to draft its statutes.
2.       During the first phase were established a dairy processing plant
(capacity 5000 liters per day) a 230 ha technical support center (CAT), a
106 ha fattening facility and a small veterinary pharmacy. Management of
these entities has so far not been satisfactory. It had been envisaged
that these farilit4es would be managed by the director of the Gishwati
component. Systematic interference in this management by the Kigali
coordination office of the first phase project has been very detrimenta&.
The dairy facility originally established no process 5000 liters a day is
barely capeble of processing 2500 liters per day due to poor technical
choices of equipment, poor organization and poor ,udgement of the Kigali
coordiratiorn office.  The IDA credit will therefore finance under PPF the
equipment necessary to bring the dairy's Lapacity to 5000 liters a day. A
secor.d sEage investment to increase the dairy's capacity to 10.000 liters a
day has also been included in the credit and will be conditional to the
result of -he lives-tork study.  -he only further investments foreseen for
