Phase I - Pasture and Dairy Development within the Forest
3.       The primary goal of pasture development within the forest zone in
Phase I was to accomodate surplus livestock estimated at 25,000 head of
Ankole cattle, grazing in the forest and causing degradation. High
productive Kikuyu grass and white clover would be established and the
existing Ankole cattle would progressively be replaced with high yielding
Brown Swiss dairy cattle. Farmers would be organized into pastoral
associations assisted by the sub-project's livestock service.
4.       By the end of Phase I more than 3,400 ha forest have been cleared,
a much larger area than the 2,000 ha envisaged at appraisal. As of
04/30/86, a total area of 3,164 ha forest have been cleared and divided
into pastures of about 30 ha each; these have been allocated to ,roups of
farmers. Of the 3,400 ha cleared forest about 3,164 ha have been planted
with Kikuyu grass and .are being stocked with cattle. In addition to the
3,164 ha pastures, there are about 336 ha pastures belonging to CAT (230
ha), and to the fattening unit (106 ha). These pastures are stocked with
about 865 head of Brown Swiss dairy cattle, 1,483 crosses between Ankole
and Brown Swiss and 3,400 Ankole cattle. This gives a total area of 3,500
ha pastures, grazed by a total herd of 5,750 head of cattle ab of September
1986. The carrying capacity for the first year's pastures is estimated at
0.3 UBT per ha, in the second year's pastures 1.5 UBT and in the third
year's pastures 2.0 UBT per ha. The total carrying capacity of Phase I
pastures is about 6,600 UBT by 1989.
5.       Pastures are developed in grazing units (blocs) of ]50 ha, each of
which are divided into 5 pastures of approximately 30 ha; a pasture of 30
ha can carry 60 b'T (1 UBT is equivalent to 250 kg live-weight), and
therefore, a grazing unit can carry 300 UBT or between 300 and 400 head of
Ankole cattle, In principle, pastures should have been allocated to a
group of farmers living In the 8 surrounding communes (Karago, Giciye,
C-aseke, Ramba, Mutara, Kanama, Kayovre and RutsIro) in accordance to the
number of cattle in each commune. However, preferences were given to
better off beneficiaries and only recently small farmers from the communes,
organized in zroups, have been allocated pastures. Pastures of the better



- 01 
- 91 -                          Annex 5
off benenficiaries are stocked mainly w:lth cows of Brown Swiss and crosses,
and pastures belonging to small farmers' groups are stocked with Ankole
cattle. The pastures were allocated by project management througn a
commission and the beneficiaries who mainly (for 60%) consists of higher
Government officials and other influential individuals including project
staff, and not small subsistence farmers, who had cattle in the forest.
Further, as these individuals in general are not owners of Ankola cattle,
it only contributed a little to the destocking of Ankole cattle, an
activity w'"ich was envisaged at appraisal. So far, destocking of Ankole
cattle has been far 'ess than anticipated and it is estimated that
aporoximately the same number of Ankole cattle remains in the forest as
before project implementation.
6.       The basic infrastrlucuture for a grazing unit consists of a
veterinary dispensary, a house for an A3 veterinarian, a cattle crush and a
bull service center. Also, pastures have been delimitated, marked and
fenced, and water points and access roads being constructed. Finally, each
grazing unit has been given a stcck of veterinary products.
7.       At the onset of the project there was no experience in the c(untry
with developing pastures in higher altitudes by planting of Kikuyu grass.
However, in northern Kivu (Massisi) this type of pasture was successfully
established 20 years ago and resulted in high yields of between 3,000 to
4,000 feeding units per ha or equivalent to a carrying capacity of 750 kg
or 3 UBT per ha which is somewhat higher than at Gishwati. The
introduction of Kikuyu grass was also successful in Gishwatl and resulted
in pastures of high productivity and of a high nutrition value for dairy
cows. It is estimated that these pastures can carry 2 UBT (Unite du Betail
Tropicale) per ha or 500 kg liveweight or equivalent to one Brown Swiss
dairy cow. In comparison, many tropical pastures have a carrying capacity
which is more than 10 tlmes lower. Furthermore, tha grass, with its deep
root system (up to 5 m), is well suited as erosion control. Also, the
climate in the high altitude of Gishwati, between 2,200 and 2,800 m, is
free of tsetse flies and ticks, thus allowing the introduction of exotic,
high-yielding dairy breeds, which are nmobt susceptible to trypanosomiasis
(a disease transmitted by tsetse flies) and tick-borne diseases. The main
drawback to establishing Kikuyu grass is the high cost due to lack of
farmers participation in land clearing and planting. Therefore, the high
cost must be offset by an intensive dairy production using exotic breeds;
this is hampered by the lack of exotic dairy cattle in Rwanda; however, the
supply of dairy cattle can be provided by import from the neighboring
northern Kivu, which has a herd of about 20,000 Brown Swiss dairy cattle.
However, the import is limited and maximum 300 breeding heifers are
available for export per year. This breed has adapted well to northern
Kivu and is also being adapted well to the Gishwati forest area; the main
problem facing the import of breeding stock from Kivu is the high incidence
if brucellosis and tuberculosis, thus a strict sanitary control is needed.
8.       Investmen!. costs for the establishment of Kikuyu pastures vary
according tD the vegetation of the forest; in the beginning land clearing
required 200K man-days per ha, plowirng 300 man-days, planting of grass 260
man-days and maintenance of pastures required 160 and 80 man-days in the
first and second year respectively. Total labor requirements amounted to
1,000 man-days per ha or RWF 100,000 (US$1,200).  Due to thie high labor



- 92 -                              Annex 5
cost, project management allowed the cultivation of potatoes previous to
planting of Kikuyu grass in order to reduce weeding costs. The labor
requiremert was then reduced from 1,000 man-days to 700 man-days.
9.       By comparison, in northern Kivu the equivalent labor requiremdent
for establishing one ha pasture is lower, about 500 man-days: 25 man-days
for land clearing, 200 man-days for plowing, and 80 man-days for planting
of grass and 200 man-days for maintenance (in year 1 and 2). Furthermore,
maintenance of pastures in northern Kivu required only one man on
permanence on 20 ha or 15 man-days per ha.
10.      In order to offset the high cost of pastures, dairy cows of high
milk potential must be stockci on these pastures. To facilitate the
purchases of such dairy cows, mainly imports from Zaire of high yielding
Brown Swiss dairy cows, the project introduced the purchase of dairy cattle
on credit. The credit period was 24 months at 4X p.a., and the ceiling per
dairy cow or heifer was set at RwF 60,000. Payments are deducted from milk
sales. So far, about 260 Brown Swiss dairy heifern have been imported from
Zaire, most of them on credit. Dairy heifers from Zaire should be
carefully selected before imported to Rwanda and a health certificate
should be a prerequisite as well as quarantin¢ of the animals.
1l.      There are only a few animal diseases which threaten the dairy
herd3 in Gishwati due to the high altitude where most tropical parasites
are .bsent. However, a major problem is bloat, which is an excessive
accumulation of gas in the ruminant stomach; it causes great discomfort and
is frequently fatal if not discovered in time.  Bloat occurs in cattle on
highly productive pastures and the incidence is high on clover-dominant
pastures which are growing rapidly, such as the case on the Gishwati
pastures.

DRAFT OVERALL TERMS OF REFERENCE
Gishwati Land Use Study And Plan
Objectives and Products
1.       The principal objective of this study is to determine the best
land uses and produce a detailed management plan for those degraded
portioas of the Gishwati forest area designated for conversion and
management. A secondary objective is to determine the extent and nature of
land development activities that have occurred during Phase I of the
Integrated Forestry, Agriculture and Livestock Project.
2.       To achieve these objectives, the project will consist of the
following specific stages'
a. Produce a map showing current land uses in the study area;
b. Produce a map showing the physical capabilities of the study
area for the various possible land uses;
C. Produce a socio-economic analysis and description of current
and potential land uses in the study erea;
d. Evaluate the relative suitability of all parts if the study
area for the various possible land uses and produce maps showing the
possible locations of various uses;
e. Select the most suitable combination of land uses for the
entire study area, and produce a detailed management plan to implement and
manage the selected land uses; and,
f. If the selected land uses are acceptable to IDA and the
Governmnent, prepare a program of further investments by IDA and other
donors to assist in manLgement of the Gishwati forest area.
3.       The study schedule calls for completion of the entire study not
later than June 1988. The phases are given below. Funding will be
p:ovided under the Project Preparation Facility for the proposed Second
Integ.ated Forestry, Agricultuire and Livestock Project.
Prolect Phases
Phase I
Phase I. Detailed Criteria and Terms of Reference.
4.       Specify Criteria.  Thc first task is to hire a consultant under
PPF financing to prepare criteria and detailed teams of reference for the
study based on the outline contained in this document. The consultant will
specify clearly the entire set of objective criteria that may be used by
the various parties for event.'ally deciding on the most suitable land uses
for the project area. These criteria will be used at the beginning of the
study as the basis for determining what additional data to gather and what
analyses and interpretation to carry out.  Criteria may include:
a. Increase milk production.
b.  Increase timber production.
c. Convert from indigenous cattle breeds to exotic breeds.
d.  Prote-t soil from erosion.



95 -                          Annex 5
Appr.ndi.x 1
Page 2
e.  Make pasture establishment cost effective.
f. Remove cattle from remaining natural forest.
g. Increase regeneration of natural forest.
h.  Increase agricultural or export crop production.
i.  Pr-ide additional pasture or agricultural land to local
residents.
J.  Ensure that local residents are the beneficiaries of further
development of the project area.
k. Develop a tourism industry in the area, based on the natural
forest and the scenic attractions of the surrounding area.
1. Protect and to the extent possible restore populations of wild
birds and mammals in the ne.tural forest.
5.       Develop Detailed Work Program for Study.  Not enough is known at
present about what information is available on the study area e.g.    _
maps, aerial photographs, satellite magery, etc. and what additional
information is required for the study. The consultant would review data
availability and determine what needed data already exists and what
additional data is needed. He would then develop detailed terms of
reference (work program) for the following.
a. Data Parameters. The task at this phase will be to specJfy
the parameters for data to be collacted on the following subjects:
-Land cover and land use (e.g., natural forest, degraded
forest/bushland, forest plantations, pasture, cultivated land, settlements,
roads/trails, livestock numbers and herd composition);
-Soils and Topography (e.g., slope, soil classification);
-Socio-Econoric (e.g., current uses of the project area,
attitudes toward changes in land use, population, family size, place of
origin;
-Farm Marketing and Pricing (e.g., market and pricing
information on major area products);
b.  Interpretation and E aluation Parameters.  The task at this
stage uill be to specify the criteria to be used in interpretation of data
and evaluation of results, for the following stages:
-Land Capability (e.g., erodibility of soils, erodibility under
various cover types and crops, *ields for various crops)
-Land Suitabilitv (e.g., cost-effectiveness of various
forestry, livestock or Dgsicultural products, ability of various land uses
to meet multi'le objectives such as efficient production plus soil
conservation, social acceptability of various land uses)
-Alternative Land Use Scenarios (e.g., maximizing the most
important of the objective criteria for deciding future land use of the
area, while minimizing undesired effects).
c. Determine Best Means o. Getting Additional Data Needed.
d. Determine Costs for Additional Data.
e. Draw Up Detailed Study Schedule.

Phase II. Data Collection.
6.       Field work will be carried out to collect the data identified in
the first phase as necessary for the project. Parameters for the various
sets of data will be specified in the first phase. Data on the following
subjects will be collected:
a.   Land Resources
1. Land cover/land use
2. Soils and topography
b. Socio-Economic
c. lzricultural Marketing and Prices
Phase III. Land Capability Analysis
7.       The physical capability of land for various uses will be~ evaluated
on the basis of the criteria developed in the first stage of the project.
Phase IV. Land Suitability Analysis
8.       The suitability of land for various uses will be evaluated o;i the
basis o a combination of physical capability, economic, and social
desirability criteria, as defined in Phase I. The evaluation will be
organized in a hierarch-, of limiting factors, such that principal
constraints on land use (e.g., steepness of slope) will be considered first
and only those areas which are not eliminated from consideration for
particular uses will be evaluated further (e.g., steep areas suitable only
for forestry will not be further considered as sites for culi.vation).
Phase V. Evaluation Land Use Alternatives
9.       The task for this stage will be to prepare approximately three
area-wide land use packages for evaluation as alternative w&ys of
developing the region. Each will emphasize to different degrees the most
important of the management objectives identified in Phase I. Each will be
presented as a map showing where various land uses would occur, a brief
description of how the land uses changes would be implemented and all land
uses would be managed, and an evaluation of the degree to which various
objectives are met or not met. Economic consequences will be quantified to
the degree poss'ble.  Packeges of possible investments by IDA and other
donors will be part of the description of how the land use altern ives
would be implemented.
Phase VI. Choose Best Land Uses
10.      The task at this stage will be to organize and tead a workshop of
Government and Bank staff to choose the preferred combination of land uses
for the region. Staff responsible for the study present the resu'.cs. The
principal criteria for selection of the preferred land use alternative will
.e the degree of achievement of important objectives. The explicit
evaluaticn of consequences of alternative land uses may lead to new
rankings of objectives.

Phase VII. Prepare Detailed Manaaement Plan
A detailed plan to guide implementation of the land uses and
decisions zrade in Phase VI on management of the area will be prepared.
Important components will be maps designating allowei land uses, and
management guidelines to direct those uses.
Management and Investment.
Following completion of the study, the following actions are anticipated
by Government and the Bank:
Manage area according to management plan.
b. IDA and other donors possibly undertake additional investments
to support managemenc if agreement is reached on proposed investment
packages.

de transfer des activitis du projet (en coordination vec leas
directeurs des deux composantes) i d'autres organism.s, ccci dans
1e but d'assurer quo co transfer so fasse dans lea mei3llur*s
conditions possibles.

RWANDA
SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
30 HA. PASTURE MODEL
FINANCIAL RATE OF RETURN 65%
Sensitivity Analysis
Decrease in milk price               Financial rate of returns
10%                                        51%
20%                                         36%
30%                                         17%
The results indicate that the financial rate of return would remain
satisfactory should the price of milk or milk production decrease by 20 to
30%. However, the farm model represents an average pasture. The financial
rate of return is very sensitive to milk production and therefore to the
speed at which pure breed cattle is introduced on each pasture. During the
first phase project some influential beneficiaries obtained preferential
treatment to receive pure breed cattle thus leaving less well-off pastures
beneficiaries without pure breed cattle. Assurances concerning equitable
pure breed cattle distribution will be sought during negotiations.

It has been agreed with the borrower that each pasture beneficiary
would slaughter at least two Ankole female for each pure breed brown swiss
Introduced in Gishwati.  The average price of an Ankole female is 252 of the
price of a pure breed cow or about FRW 12450.  By selling two ankole for each
pure breed cattle the pasture beneficiaries are able to finunce about 50% of the
purchase price of a pure breed cow thus leaving him with a balance of 50%. It
has been estimated that this credit could be reimbursed in 2 yearP.
Years
1           2           3         4        5          6
Amount borrowed           149400      199200      49800    12430    12450          -
Debt Service              74700      174300      124500    37350       5602       1867
Principal interest       22410        4108.;      22410      7470    24900      12450
T'otal                   97110       215385      146910    44820    30502       14317
5.          Arikole Cattle.
After pasture establishment it has been estimated that 60 UBT or
15000 kg of cattle could be introduced on the pasture.  The average weight of an
ankole cattle in its traditional environment is about 250 kg. The pasture
bereficiaries can therefore introduce about 60 ankole females at an average unit
price of FRW 12450 or a total amount of FRW 747000. The ankole cattle will
subsequently be slaughtered as the pure breed cattle is progressively
introduced.
6.        Pasture upkeep.
Past experience has demonstrated that pastures require substantial
maintenar,ce during the two years following their establishment.  This
maintenance is 66.6 man days per hectare per year at a manday cost of FRW 100.0
thus resulting in a total yearly cost of FRW 200,000 per year for a 30 ha
pasture. Since the pasture cannot generate sufficient revenues in year 1 to



- l1s -
cover pasture maintenance it is paid by the project and stbsequently recovered
through the cost recovery mechanism. This cost is however born by the pasture
beneficiairies from year 2 onwards. Pasture maintenance has been estimated at
the following levels for year 3 to 20:
Years
3-8        9-20
Mandays/ha/year                          20           10
Manday cost FRW                       100         100
Cost per Ha FRW                      2000        1000
30 Ha.                                 30          30
Cost per pasture FRW                 60,000      30,000
7.          Salaries.
Each pasture employs 3 herders and one pasture manager. It has been
estimated that one A2 level veterinarian was necessary per group of 15 pastures.
The pasture beneficiaries have to support this cost starting in the fourth year
following pasture establishment. It is also necessary to employ two assistant
veterinarians per group of 5 pastures. This cost will be born 13y the project in
year 1 and by the pasture beneficiaries thereafter. The table below is a
summary of the resulting costs for each pasture.
Years

FRW                312000       351600       351600       368160
8.       Veterinary products.
It was assumed that the expense for ieterinary products would amount to
FRW 2003 per pure breed animal 2 years old and older. This amount is detailed
below.
FRW
Meteorization (bloat) twice a year         1000
Antibiotics (Mastitis) once per year        500

Foot and mouth disease                      100
Artificial insemination                      55

- 119 -
9.       Mineral salt
It was estimated that ad..' animals 2 years old and over would consume
20 kg. of mineral salt per year at a cost of FRW 32.5/kg.
10.      Rental fee.
There is a five year grace period to allow pastures to constitute their
berds, before the cost recovery mechanism is in effect. The cost recovery
mechanism is composed of three elements as follows:
i. all investment costs related to pasture establishment until the end
of 1986
ii. all investment costs related to pasture establishment from January
1, 1987 to December 30, 1989 (until the federation of livestock
owners is created).
iii. the yearly cperating cost of the federation starting on January 1,
1990.
The mission's calculations indicate that FRW 767.0 million have been invested in
pasture establishment up to the end of 1986. Poor management of the component
during phase 1 resulted in higher costs than anticipated representing a
substantial financial burden on the pastures. As a result of this full cost
recovery on current IBRD lending terms (interest rate 7.92%; repayment period of
20 years including 5 years grace) would jeopardize the financial viability of
the pastures. For this reason FRW 381.0 million (50% of the total investment
cost) is being considered as a government subsidy. The balance of FRW 386.0
millioz. has been considered a long term debt bearing a 7.92% interest rate, a
repayment period of 20 years including a 5 years grace period. This represents
a recovery of FRW 396,000 for a 30 ha pasture per year (item i). It is
estimated that a further FRW 120.0 million will be spent to complete the
pastures by 1989 (item ii)which will be subject to the terms of the cost
recovery mechanism described above, thus resulting in a recovery of FRW 125,000
per year for a 30 ha pasture. The yearly operating cost of the federation is
estimated to be about FRW 6.0 million per year starting in 1990. This cost
divided by the 3340 ha of pasture in operation would result in a fee of FRW 1800
per hectare per year or FRW 54000 per 30 hectare per year (item iii). Adding
these three figures results in a total amount of FRW 575,000 per year for a 30
hectare pasture starting in year 6.
11.      Communal Cattle Tax
The communes collect a RWF250 tax on all adult animals. This tax
results in the following amounts per year:
1     2      3     4      5     6      7     8  9-20

FRW          15000 13500 10250  9750  4000  3750  3750  4500  4500

CENTRE D'APPUI TECHNIQUE (CAT)
1.       Milk Sales
Yearly milk production multiplied by FRW45 per liter
(constant until end 1989, increased yearly for inflation
thereafter.)
2.       Meat Sales
Number of animals sold multipliec by respective weights
niultiplied by FRW55 per kilo (constant price until end 1989,
followed by 3% yearly increase for inflation from 1990-1992).
3.       Salaries
Actual 1986 level multiplied by 3% a year over the projected
period. Salaries are not adjusted for inflation in Rwanda;
are only increased by 3% every year.
4.       Transport
Actual 1986 figure multiplied by 10% in 1987 and 1988 and by
7% over the remaining projected period (this includes an
adjustment for inflation of 3% in 1987, 6% in 1988, 5% in
1989, 3% from 1990-1992).
5.       Tools
Same as above.
6.       Veterinary Products
Average number of pure breed heads (2 years ani older)
multiplied by FRW 2000 per head in 1986 multiplied yearly by
the inflation factor over the projected period.
7.       Cattle Feed
Milking cows
85   86  87  88   89   90   91   92

producing period) multiplied by FRW 30 per kilo infl2ted for

9.       Interest and amortization:
a)   Given the fact that the CAT is only starting to build its
herd in 1987, it has not been possible to consider its
establishment cost as a long term debt to the Government.  It
has been assumed that out of the FRW 97.7 million spent to
establish the CAT, FRW 50.0 million were Government equity
contributions. The balance (FRW 47.7 million) was considered
as a long-term deJt to the Government, bearing a 7.922
interest rate, a 5 year grace period and a 20 year repayment
period. The unpaid interest during the grace period was
added to the principal and to gross fixed assets.

II. Sources and Uses of Funds
1.   Debt service
It was assumed that all investments made under the first credit (1039-
RW) would be a debt to the Government with an interest rate of 7.92% a
grace period of 5 years, a repayment period of 20 years, and interest
added to principal and gross fixed assets. It was assumed that investments
included in the upcoming IDA credit would be transferred to the dairy as
Government equity contributions.
III. Balance Sheet
1. Inventories
4 months supplies + chemicals and office supplies.
2. Accounts Receivable
1 month of sales.
3. Accounts Payable
1 month purchase of milk, energy, chemicals and supplies.

2.   Cost of Sales
The veterinary pharmacy uses a 25% margin. Therefore cost of sales =
sales/1.25.
3.   Salaries
Inflated by 3% every year.

1986 figure inflated thereafter by 3% in 1987, 6% in 198e, 5% in 1989,
3% in 1990-1992.

This was done by the dairy in 84/85. In 1986 1 trip to Kigali (320
km.) in subsequent years 2 trips per year to Kigali (640 Km) per year.
Inflated every year for inflation (32 in 1987, 6% in 1988, 5% IN 1989,
32 IN 1990-1992).
6.   Maintenance
1.5% of building value per year inflated 'or inflation every year (3%
in 1987, 6% IN 1988, 5% IN 1989, 3% IN 1990-1992).
7,   Depreciation
Depreciated at 2% per year thus assuming a 50 year life for the
Building.

I.   Source and uses of funds
1.   Debt service
Given the tight financial situation of the veterinary pharmacy, it was
not possible to consider all the investments necessary for its
creation as a debt to the government. For this reason it was assumed
that FRW 2.77 million was a government equity contribution. However,
the remaining investment cost (FRW 3.0 million) was considered as a
long term cebt bearing a 7.92% interest rate, a grace period of 5
years and a repayment period of 20 years. Unpaid interest during the
grace period was added to the principal and to gross fixed assets.
III Balance Sheet
1.   Inventories
3 months of stock of veterinary products and 3 months of office
furniture.
2.   Accounts Receivable
1 month of sales
3.   Accounts payable
3 weeks of purchases

- 143 -                           Annex 8
FORESTRY COMPONENTS
Cost recovery will come mainly from the sale of wood, although a
small proportion is also expected to come from the sale of seedlings. As
regards the wood, it is proposed that the annual yield from each plantation
should be4sold standing (stumpage) to the highest bidder, rather than at
some fixed statutory stumpage rate which would be the same for all
plantations irrespective of their accessibility, and which would also tend
to lag behind the development of the free-market price. Only if the sale
of the standing wood does not attract sufficient bids, e.g. if
entrepreneurs generally do not have the means of doing the logging, should
the Forestry Department consider doing this operation itself, and then sell
the wood on roadside in the plantations.
Most seedlings in Rwanda are produced by the joint efforts of the
umuganda and the Forestry Department, and a reasonable quantity per family
per year is then freely put at the disposal of tne umuganda members in
return for their labor and for the taxes which they pay. Some, however,
are sold to non-umuganda members or to umuganda members who need more than
their quota. It is naturally difficult to foresee what proportion of total
seedling production will be sold, but it has here been estimated at 20%.
I. FROM SALE OF WOOD
A. KIGALI-BUTARE FUELWOOD AND POLE PLANTATIONS
The productive area of these Eucalyptus plantations is estimated
at 5,215 ha. It is envisaged that the first fellings will be carried out
in 1991, when the oldest plantations are ten years old. The volume yield
is expected to increase from some 13,600 m3 in 1991 to an average of about
45,000 m3 p.a. in year 2006, and beyond. Altogether some 788,344 m3 would
be produced between 1991 and 2006.
The plantations would produce fuelwood and poles. Most of the
fuelwood would be converted to charcoal, which would be sold in the towns
of Kigali and Butare. Most of the poles would be used for the construction
of traditional housing; but scaffolding, fence posts and even some
telephone poles from the occasional good sites, are other potential
markets.
Although the free-market stumpage price for fuelwood, in Rwanda as
elsewhere, is generally zero in the most remote supply areas, it has
reached a level of about RF 1,000/m3 near Kigali, while it is about RF
500/m3 about 50 km from Kigali. Here it has been assumed that the average
today is RF 750/m<. It has further been assumed that 10% of the wood will
be sold as poles at RF 4,000/m3, giving a weighted average of RF 1,075/m3.



- 144 -                             Annex 8
The resulting expected revenues from the Kigali-Butare plantations
are set out in the table at the end of this section.
B. NYUNGWE BUFFER PLANTATION
The 900 ha of the Nyungwe buffer strip plantation, along the
southeastern boundary of the Nyungwe natural forest, would be made up of
pine, cypress, acacia, and indigenous species. Among the latter would be
Polyscias fulva ("Umwungo"), a fast-growing pioneer species with excellent
form and for which there is a good market at the match factory in Butare.
T'he main products, however, would be poles and sawtimber.
It is expected that production would start with 2,250 m3 p.a. from
1997 to 2002, increase to 4,500 m3 p.a. from 2003 to 2007, and eventually
to 58,500 m3 p.a. from year 2028 to 2031. The stumpage price of poles in
the Nyungwe region is expected to be RF 3570 per m3, while that of sawlogs
and matchwood is conservatively estimated at RF 2,532 per m3. For total
expected benefits, see table at end of section.
C. GISHWATI
Phase I Plantations
The volume yields (set out in the economic analysis table) are
expected to start in 1990 with 288 m3 of poles with a standing value of RF
4000/nm3. The first sawlog production, 7800 mn3 valued at RF 2978/m3, is
expected in 1994. Due to increasing supply of pine sawtimber, its price is
expected to decrease by 2% p.a. from RF3500/m3 in 1986 to RF2860 in 1996,
and then level off at that price. These are financial prices, in 1986
terms. The resulting revenues are set out in the table at the end of this
section, while the corresponding volume yields are set out in the economic
analysis tables.
Phase II Plantations
The expected volume productions are set out in the economic
analysis table, the expected prices are set out in the previous paragraph,
and the corresponding revenues are set out in the table at the end of this
note.
Buffer Plantation
The main role of the proposed 200-ha buffer plantation would be to
protect the natural forest reserve, and it is not proposed to do any short-
rotation felling in it. The first fellings may take place at 30 years,
i.e. after the period whith which we are here concerned.
II. FROM SALE OF SEEDLINGS
A. KIUGALI-BUTARE



- 145 -                             Annex 8
As set out in the working paper on the Kigali-Butare nursery
program, the proposed seedling production from the Sector nurseries would
increase from 420,000 in the first year of the project, to 2.1 million in
the fifth and following years. Most of these seedlings will be distributed
to the umuganda members in return for their labor, but it is expected that
about 20% will be sold. The cost recovery from these sales would rise from
RF 0.2 million in year 1 to RF 1 million in year 5 - see table at end of
this section.
B. GISHWATI: THREE PROJECT COMMUNES OUTSIDE THE FOREST
The existing 24 nurseries would continue to be supported. They
produce 720,000 seedling per year. As in the case of the Kigali-Butare
nurseries, most of the seedlings are produced maiily by the umuganda for
the umuganda, and only aboui 202 are expected to be sold. The cost
recovery is expected to be RF 0.3 mill. p.a.. See working paper for
details.

SECOND INTEGRATED FORESTRY PROJECT
Economic Analysis
Economic Analysis of the Kigali/Butare Fuelwood Plantations
1.       The analysis of this component has been divided into three parts:
(a) a 'base case' analysis which looks at the incremental costs
and benefits implied by the proposed investment, the key assumptions of
which are as follows4 (i) the project's wood is essentially intended to
meet the fuel needs of urban dwellers, which needs are at present largely
met by charcoal burning; (ii) a 'with' project situation which includes an
administrative support structure that has been scaled down from that which
prevailed under the first phase (see Working Paper         ; (iii) a 'without"
project situation that involves a more rapid phasing out of the GBK
administrative unit than proposed under the 'with' project situation and
only 'minimal' maintenance (comprising the upkeep of track and firebreaks
and essential investments in extraction roads/paths); and (iv) a 'without'
project situation in which yields from the 'newer' (1984-86) plantations
are substantially more adversely affected by the lack of maintenance than
the older plantations, with an average yield reduction of 751 for all
plantations;
(b) a straightforward 'cost-benefit' analysis of the project's
costs and benefits which ignores what would happen in the absence of the
project;
(c) a similar 'cost-benefit' analysis for the 'without' project
situation in which the key assumption is that no costs are incurred other
than those essential to wood extraction 'i.e., extraction path
construction/maintenance).
Prolect Benefits
2.       The price of wood has been based on the assumed response of urban
dwellers to a situation in which charcoal was no longer available. Various
alternatives have been reviewed, ranging from possible usage of briquets,
of gas, of ele;tricity and of kerosene.  Following the findings of the
Bank's (ESMAP) Report on 'Improved Charcoal Cookstove Strategy' (dated June
1986), the burning of briquets has been taken as the 'base case'
alternative to charcoal. Assuming urban dwellers would continue to use, at
least in the short run, their charcoal stoves, the economic price of
fuelwood in terms of its briquet alternative is estimated at FRW 1835 per
m3 solid (See Table 1).
3.       The eatimated economic price of fuelwood needs to be seen against
the present 'official' price of plantation wc,d of FRW 800 per m3 solid and
a market price of charcoal which converts into a wood price of
approximately FRW 300 per m3 solid. The former price is essentially
academic as nobody buys wood at that price; the latter reflects the fact
that fuelwood is at present almost a 'free' commodity, as wood for charcoal
making purposes is largely collected free from the savannah regicns.
4.       The mission reviewed the possibility of using the replacement cost
of wood provided by 'private' farmers in the rural areas, which is largely



- 148 -                                Annex 9
by way of agroforestry rather than by way of private woodlots/plantations.
This approach to the economic valuation of fuelwood was rejected on the
grounds that wood from this source could not reasonably be expected to
provide sufficiently and reliably for the needs of urban dwellers. Yet
another alternative, of pricing fuelwood according to the replacement cost
of wood from state plantations, was also rejected on the grounds that such
an analysis would be self-serving since the GBK Phase I plantations provide
the only effective data base on which to estimate such replacement cost.
(On this basis, wood would be priced at least at FRW 2,700 per m3 solid).
5.       For completeness, the economic price of fuelwood based on the next
most plausible alternative for charcoal after briquets; i.e. kerosene is
also calculated. The estimated price here is FRW 1280 per i3.

Economic Analysis of Industrial Wood Plantations
7.       Two separate investments have been analyzed although the
assumptions with regard to each one are essentially the same. These
investments are (i) the maintenance of the Gishwati Phase I plantations;
and (ii) the establishment of 2,000 ha of new plantations at Gishwati. The
principal objective of tEase components is 'protection' of the natural
forest. The benefits of protection are essentially unquantifiable,
especially so in the case of Rwanda where data pertaining to the adverse
impact of natural forest destruction on watershed management and soil
erosion and the resultant adverse (economic) consequences is largely
lacking. The economic analysis includes only those benefits and costs
which are quantifiable.
8.       The economic analysis of tbe new plantations is based on a
straightforward cost/benefit approach, and with the maintenance of the
Phase I Gishwati Plantation, an 'incremental' analysis has been made.
Prolect Benefits
9.       The price of wood has been taken at its present market value but
adjusted to reflect a likely decrease in the real price over the period to
the end of this century. This contrasts with the more normal assumption
made in forestry project analysis of real price increases for sawnwood over
the project period. However, although reliable data on the supply and
demand situation is unavailable, in the case of Rwanda, existing projects
are likely to push the country into an excess supply over demand situation
within the next few years. In these circumstances, and in the absence of
realistic export markets for Rwandese industrial sawnwood, the economic
analysis, in addition to applying the standard conversion factor (0.85) to
the present market price, has assumed a 2% real price decrease per year
over the ten-year period 1988-1997. After that, present Government efforts
to expand demand, coupled with a slowdown in the present rate of supply
expansion, can perhaps be assumed to cause prices to stabilize. Under
these assumptions, the price of sawnwood is expected to decrease from its
present (economic) price of FRW 2978/M3 standing, (see Table 2) to FRW
2433/m3 standing by 1997.  The price of poles, at FRW 3400/m3, has been
taken at its present market value but adjusted by the standard conversion
factor.
10.      The economic price of sawnwood as estimated is somewhat higher
than the stumpage fee that would be required to cover the cost of
replacement (assuming a 10% discount rate) which is estimated at FRW 1758
(see Table 2). Set against these values, and indeed against the actual
market price itself, the present 'official' price of FRW 5700 per m3 could
be substantially reduced. (Of course the market situation varles between
species, but at present there exists little data with which to effectively
distinguish particular prices appropriate to different species).

Economic Analysis of Gishwati Pastures
12.      The analysis of the Gishwati pasture component has been divided
into two parts, (a) an analysis of the proposed maintenance of the pastures
established in Phase I and (b) an analysis of the proposal to invest in
2000 ha of additional pastures under Phase II.
13.      A.  Phase I pasture mai-itenance.  In addition to a straightforward
'cost/benefit' analysis of this compontent, an 'incremental' analysis



1 -                              A__nnex 9
Page 5
comparing a 'without' project scenario as against the assumed 'with'
project case has been made. Although the 'without' scenario is naturally
more difficult to predict than the 'with' project case and therefore the
incremental analysis is here taken as the 'base-case'. The following key
assumptions have been made: (i) all Phase I milk is sold processed and
therefore, (ii) investments to bring the dairy up to a capacity of 10,000
litres per day have been included, as have the costs of necessary periodic
replacements over the 30 year period of ana'lysis; (iii) in addition the
direct post project direct costs of maintaining and operating the pastures,
the cost of operating a 'Federation of Pasture Owners' to manage the scheme
has been included for the post project period; and (iv) phase I period
investment costs are counted as sunk and therefore as irrelevant costs.
14.      'Without' the project substantial overstocking of the pastures has
been assumed for the earlier years, leading to pasture degradation and an
eventual 'steady state' milk production equal to about 50% of the estimated
'with' project production. Another key assumption is that without the
project the proposed investme-- to bring the dairy up to a capacity of 4000
litres per day would be made but not the subsequent investment to reach
10,000 litres per day. Therefore, any milk produced in excess of 4,000
litres per day is assumed to be sold at a discount to the estimated
economic price of processed milk.
15.      The price of milk has been based on the import parity pr'ce of
powdered milk. Project milk can be assumed to substitute for present
actual powdered milk imports, at present the only realistic alternative to
Rwandese, domestically produced milk. The import parity price is based on
quotes obtained from the New Zealand Dairy Board and is estimated at FRW 24
per litre for processed milk and FRW 18.8 per litre for unprocessed milk.
(See Table 4).
16.      The New Zealand quotes have been compared to EEC quoted prices and
found to be equivalent.
17.      The price of meat has been taken at its theoretical export parity
price of FRW 93.2/kg. The present market price of meat is set by
Government at (on average) FRW 57/kg (liveweight) and reflects a number of
depressing factors including the effect brought about by illegal imports
from Uganda during the time of its recent troubles. Data on the supply and
demand situation for meat products in Rwanda is very weak but it appears
that the impact of the project is more likely to push meat into becoming an
export commodity than the 'without project' situation causing it to become
an import commodity.
18.      The export party price is assumed to be equivalent to the price
quoted in a proposed contract for the supply of meat to Congo and to
Djibouti by Rwanda. The economic price is established at FRW 93.2/kg as
follows:
Contract price          - US$1.98/kg (carcasses)
= US$0.99/kg (live weight)

20.      (B) Phase II Pasture Establishment.  The analysis here has been
divided into four parts. Common to each, however, is the assumption that
the Phase I model of pasture development would apply.
21.      (a)  The 'base case' analysis of the proposed investment in a
second phases of pasture development includes the following key assumption;
(i) Phase I and Phase II pastures effectively constitute a single scheme
and therefore the produce of Phase I is included in the benefit streams of
Phase II and, therefore, (ii) the operating costs (of pastures, CAT, Zone
d'Embouche and dairy) associated with producing Phase I benefits are
included in the cost streams of Phase II, (iii) all Phase II investment
costs are included in the analysis, as are the investment period costs
proposed for maintaining Phase I, however, (iv) investment costs already
incurred in establishing Phase I pasture are counted as sunk and therefore
irrelevant costs.

(b) As a variant to (c) ebove, an 'incremental' analysis has been
conducted in which the incremental project scenario assumed with regard to
the analysis of the Phase I maintenance component has been included in the
analysis.
(c) Another analysis was undertaken to evaluate the second phase
as a purely marginal investment, in which the key assumptions made were:
(i) that the only relevant costs are those associated with the produce of
Phase II and that therefore, (ii) the cost of bringing the dairy up to a
capacity of 10,000 litres/day is excluded on the grounds that it is
effectively an additional Phase I investment cost, but (iii) the cost of
bringing the dairy from a capacity of 10,000 litres to 18,000 litres/day is
included; (iv) Phase I pasture produce is not included in the Phase II
benefit streams and neither are the associated operating costs; and (v) the
CAT and Zone d'Embouche would continue to operate fully to service Phase I
pastures regardless of whether or not a Phase II goes ahead and therefore
their operating costs are counted as irrelevant to Phase II.
(d) As an alternate to (c) above, the assumption was made that
only Phase I milk would be sold in processed form (i.e. an investment to
bring the dairy up to a capacity of 10,000 litres a day would be made but
not one bringing it to 18,000 litres/day.

23.      Analy>  s (c) and (d) demonstrate Phase II to be an unattractive
investment in itself but when taken together with Phase I (Analysis (a) and
(b)) marginal viability is indicated. This suggests that economies of
scale apply with additional pasture establishment leading to improved rates
of return.
24.      With regard to the 'base case' analysis, the sensitivity analysis
indicates the ERR to be substantially sensitive to a change in benefits and
especially so to changes in benefits from milk production. The ERR is
equally sensitive to cost change and more so to operating costs (post
project) than to investment cost changes. Analysis (c) and (d) also
demonstrate that the project would be better off selling unprocessed rather
than processed milk.
Extension Activities in 3 Communes Outside Gishwati
25.      The analysis of this component estimates the number of
beneficiaries (farms) that would have to participate in the project in
order to yield a 15% (economic) rate of return to the proposed investments.
26.      Three farm models have been constructed each with the following
key characteristics:
(i)   Model 1, or 0.6 ha farm on which, in addition to improved
farming methods and a reorganization of land use, 100 new
trees would be planted under the project;
(ii) Model 2, a 0.8 ha farm engaged in similar activities to
Model 1 but on which, in addition to new trees planted, 3
she-goats are reared;
(iii) Model 3, a 1.3 ha farm on which an additional 150 trees are
planted and the farmer keeps one cow.
27.      Table 6 provides details of the project's overall impact on the
three model farms.
28.      Market prices have been applied for all benefits except milk.
Prices of agricultural produce are included in Table 6 (which also details
the yield assumption employed per crop). Milk has been valued at FRW 18.8
per litre reflecting the estimated economic value of unprocessed milk.
Meat, for the purposes of this analysis, is conservatively estimated at the
present average market price, i.e. FRW 55/kg and wood has been valued at
its estimated replacement cost of FRW 225 per m3.
29.      The results of the analysis indicates that approximately 5,600
farms would have to participate in and enjoy the assumed benefits from the
project in order for the proposed investments to yield a 15% rate of



Annex 9
Page 9
return. This amount represents 17% of all families in the three communes
in question and appears a conservative and achievable target for the
project. (The analysis assumes that the number of beneficiaries would
reach the target amount by the end of the 7th year from the beginning of
the project). Of the 5,600 beneficiaries, 22% are assumed to be of Model 1
type, 68% of Model 2 and 10% of Model 3 type.
Seedling Production Program
30.      The analysis of the proposed investments in this program (totaling
$0.274 m; see Working Paper No.        for details) estimates the survival
rate of seedlings produced under the program (approximately 7.8 million)
required to yield a 15% (economic) rate of return to the proposed
investment.
31.      For the price of wood the present average market price (in the
rural areas for fuelwood) of FRW 500 per m3 was used. A further analysis
was made employing the estimated replacement cost of wood, FRW 225 per m3.
Other alternative methods of valuing rural fuelwood were also considered
e.g. (i) valuing fuelwood according to the yield improvement benefits
obtained from fuelwood production enabling farmers to use agricultural crop
residues for mulching purposes rather than burning them for fuel needs;
(ii) evaluating the impact of erosion prevention on crop yields consequent
on the practice of farm torestry. However, each of these other alternative
methods of estimating the 'true' economic value of wood, (which would
almost certainly result in substantially high wood valuations), in the
absence of reliable data would require undue arbitrary assumptions.

the required survival rate to be a mere 24%

Annex 9
Table 13
Estimation of Conversion Factors
Standard Conversion Factor  1/   - 0.85
Shadow exchange rate factor 1/   - 1.18
Shadow Wage Rate 2/   - FRW 74/day; wage conversion factor   0.69
Category                        %FE                    CF
Vehicles                          80                   1.11
Equipment                         75                  1.09
Dairy Equipment                   90                  1.15
Buildings                         40                  0.98
Civil Works 3/                    10                  0.74
Seeds                             50                  1.02
Furniture                         45                  1.00
Studies                           90                  1.15
Animals                           80                   1.11
Animal Health                     75                   1.10
Salaries                           0                  0.85
Fuel                              90                  1.15
Building Maintenance              45                  1.00
Equipment Maintenance             25                  0.94
Vehicles Maintenance              40                  0.98
Civil Works Maintenance           10                  0.74
Office Administration             50                  1.02
Note3:
1/  Source:  East  Ad So_thern Africa Country Program Division.
2/ In various locations the mission found off-farm labor being employed on
farms during harvest and planting time at FRW 85 per day. In both Gishwati and
the surrounding a-eas of Kigali and Butare the GBK project has had to pay a
premium over the minim-m wage rate (FR100/day) of 10%. Assuming (i) labor
employed by project to be employed 50% of the time during harvest/planting time
and 50% at off peak time; (ii) The shadow wage rate (SWR) at peak time to be
equal to going wage (FR 85/day) paid at that time and that at off peak to be
half the minimum wage (i.e., Fr 50/day) and (iii) that the 10% premium paid by
the project be applicable to the SWR. The overall SWR is
(85 x .5) + (50 x .5)1.1 - FRW 74.
31 80% of civil works are assumed to comprise labor paid at the financial rate
cf FR 110 per day. The remaining 20% is assumed to comprise equipment and
materials of which 502 is assumed to be foreign exchange.
